5.1.6
Discussion
All graphs showing the aggregated production of a given fuel type have three component.
First the blue area represent the range within which the aggregated committed capacity
could operate if no ramping constraints were assigned to the power plants.
This is
delimited by a maximum being the aggregated committed capacity and a minimum
being the aggregated minimum power output level. Second the black line represents
the real aggregated production calculated. To finish with the red area stands for the
possible up and down deviations at the following hour. It highlights the inter-temporal
constraint that ramping represent for the power plants and are therefore shifted of one
hour (the red area is 1 hour ahead of the black curve). The ramping is not a binding
constraint for the problem for the most flexible technologies such as peat, municipal
waste, or biogas for instance. However it should be specified that these plots show the
aggregated production and constraints (blue and red areas) for each fuel type and for
the entire energy system. Different technologies can compose the mix of a given fuel
type, meaning that CCGT or simple GCT can be both using the same fuel but different
ramping constraints as reminded in Table 8. Therefore the constraints are not binding in
some cases but might actually be a limiting factor from some power plants that compose
the mix of a given fuel type. The first analysis that can be made is that the calculated
production profile follows the constraints given to the model by always being between the
blue and red area which are the capacity boundaries as well as the technical restriction
given to the power plants. This validates that the implementation of the UC through
[Jonghe, Hobbs, and Belmans 2011] was properly done and that power plants behave
accordingly to the operational constraints that were assigned to them.
Having a closer look at weeks 3 and 4 that have a high demand profile but different
wind signals Figure 23 show that prices development follow the expected path. High
prices occurs in both the situations with less spikes during week 4 due to the higher share
of wind. The link between price spikes frequency and the wind profile of these weeks
can be made by correlating the previously mentioned prices plots to the corresponding
wind signals given in Figure 21 and Figure 22. This is in line with former study of wind
power injected into the system and their effect on prices - [Jonghe, Hobbs, and Belmans
2011]. Due to the higher share of wind in week 4 the overall committed capacity is
less than for week 4. Figure 24, Figure 25 and Appendix in Section A.7 exposes the
production level for different fuel types. Base load power plants such as coal, municipal
waste, nuclear and hydro power plants are operating at maximum or relatively high
levels. It is straight forward that this is because of high demand level during these
weeks. Expensive power plants are activated such as straw, natural gas or peat. Making
the link between prices and production levels show that the price spikes in Finland for
both weeks are correlated with the production levels of natural gas and peat which seem
to be the marginal producers acting as the peakers. Denmark seem to have price spikes
related to the straw production level. Norway has very stable prices due to its high
installed capacity of hydro power plants.
Weeks 26 and 27 also follow the expected price development when comparing the
==PAGE:82==
56
5 Model extensions
calculated prices from Figure 23 with the wind and demand data shown in Figure 21 and
Figure 22. Prices are overall lower due to the small load during these weeks. The same
analysis as for weeks 3 and 4 regarding the total committed capacity can be made with a
lower value for week 27 due to its larger share of wind available. Base load technologies
(nuclear, coal etc) are still producing at high levels but not always their maximum and
again this relates to the low demand. Power plants using natural gas, straw or wood
pellets are never activated showing that the marginal producer has changed compared
with the weeks with higher demand. The mix has moved toward cheaper technologies
and this is reflected in the calculated price profile as well (overall lower prices). In week
27 price spikes in Denmark and Finland occur and are respectively related to wood chips
and peat power plants production. In week 26 Denmark seem to be subject to biogas for
its price spikes which is a cheaper technology than wood chips. This difference between
both weeks for Denmark lies in the wind signal higher for week 27 and the large installed
capacity of wind power in that country.
The scatter plots showed in Appendix in Section A.7 are in line with the previously
discussed results. Prices diversity and order of magnitude are depending on the demand
level and the associated wind signal for the different weeks.
Overall prices are very
low for the weeks with low demand due to the rough assumption made on the hydro
coefficient.
RMSE and R2 values shown in Table 12 and Table 13 respectively can be compared
to the corresponding indicators of the base model reported in Table 14 and Table 15.
Overall UC modelling has positively impacted on both indicators. However due to the
small time span considered together with the high number of assumption made for the
UC data as well as for the hydro coefficient, these results will not be further discussed.
5.1.7
Conclusion
It has been shown that UC was properly implemented by observing the production
profile of the different technologies respecting the inter-temporal constraints that were
assigned to them (ramping). Prices dynamics have shown to be satisfactory respect
with the demand level and the wind signal for the 4 different specific weeks analyzed
and are consistent with former studies correlating wind power with unit commitment
such as [Tuohy et al. 2009]. Unlike ramping, minimum up and down time could not be
investigated as expected due to the relatively small time horizon considered. Processing
time has been a limiting factor for the UC as it is addressed in [Wright 2013] and did
not allow a simulation for an entire year to assess properly whether or not it positively
contributes to the model accuracy. The objective of properly implementing a MILP
has been achieved together with a better understanding of linear pricing in this kind
of non-convex and non-continous problem. Relevant modelling knowledge have been
acquired that are specific tot his type of programming which was useful for general better
understanding of mathematical modelling of energy system. Due to the impossibility
to run a simulation for an entire year and compare it with the base case it has been
decided to not consider UC for the final version of the proposed model. This is probably
==PAGE:83==
5.1 Unit commitment
57
in adequacy with the fact that in Nord Pool the TSO‚Äôs in charge of the market clearing
do not consider the UC unlike in the US for instance - [Martin, M√ľller, and Pokutta
2012]. The goal of this project is to match the historical prices with the ones calculated
by the model, therefore including UC is probably a waste of processing time that would
not lead to better results according to the previous statement.
==PAGE:84==
58
5 Model extensions
5.2
Demand response
5.2.1
Literature survey
Over the past decade growth in electricity consumption, increase in intermittent power
sources, decline in technology cost and awareness of the importance of demand-side
management in energy markets have put demand response under the spot light in Europe
[US department of Energy 2006]. Consumers being flexible through DR programs are
likely to face lower electricity prices over the year saving money on their energy bill
[Albadi and El-Saadany 2007], [Jazayeri et al. 2005], [Kirschen 2003]. Various studies
investigate consumer‚Äôs behaviour in response to price signals [Corradi et al. 2013], [Dorini,
Pinson, and Madsen 2013]. There are different three different popular ways of modelling
DR [Andersen et al. 2006]. First, peak shaving where demand is cut at critical hours,
this occurs only few times a year when prices are high (transmission contingencies,
generator or line tripping, excessive demand).
Second, load shifting where demand
is moved on a daily basis from high priced to lower priced periods. Third, valley filling
where the consumption is increased when prices are low. DR programs have shown to
increase social welfare as well as lowering the price levels and volatility of electricity
prices [Rassenti, Smith, and Wilson 2002], [Andersen et al. 2006].
5.2.2
Description and expectations
The demand data used as input to the model are historical and therefore might include
some flexibility.
Modelling DR aims at observing whether or not some flexibility is
already included (hidden) in the demand data used and how consumers flexibility in
term of energy consumption would affect the system. Including own price elasticity (őĶt)
is a simple way of monitoring the dynamic changes on prices and consumption profiles
when the system is given some additional degree of flexibility. This parameter will be
tuned reflecting different ranges of flexibility. Higher flexibility is expected to lead to
higher social welfare, lower prices with less spikes for consumers, lower system cost and a
better use of the available resources. The degree of flexibility given through the own-price
elasticity coefficient should be chosen accordingly to a realistic degree of flexibility that
could be adopted by the consumers. DR is expected to contribute in model accuracy by
decreasing the mean price of electricity. Prices that were found to be high in the base
model. Obviously it will not contribute to better represent the extreme prices (positive
and negative) due its price ‚ÄĚflattering‚ÄĚ effect.
5.2.3
Mathematical formulation and constraints description
5.2.3.1
Elastic demand function
This subsection aims at giving the reader a better insight of how an elastic demand
function is created to model consumers consumption adjustment under a real-time
pricing scheme following the same methodology as in [De Jonghe et al. 2011]. This
methodology is thoroughly explained for one hour for better understanding and clarity.
==PAGE:85==
5.2 Demand response
59
To begin with, the base case model with totally inflexible demand levels is solved for the
entire time horizon considered with an hourly resolution. Electricity prices (marginal
price of electricity) for every time-step are thereafter extracted based on the dual of the
nodal balance equation being unchanged from the base case (See Eq.(4.2) in Section 4.1
). In order to give consumers the ability of being flexible in response to prices, a reference
is needed which will be the starting point of the elastic demand function creation. The
hourly prices calculated while optimizing the model with fixed demand levels are used
to calculated the levelized cost of energy, better known as fixed or single tariff denoted
by ŌÄ0
n. This is the price of electricity that non-flexible consumers and not subject to
a real-time pricing scheme are charged with.
Used along with the inelastic demand
levels (Dnt) the pair forms the market equilibrium (ŌÄ0
n, Dnt) at node n at hour t. This
equilibrium is afterwards used as a reference; reference around which the fixed demand
(vertical curve) will be tilted resulting in a slope change. The later is enforced through:
dnew

= Dnt + őĶnt

‚ąÄn ‚ąąN, ‚ąÄt ‚ąąT.

According to [De Jonghe et al. 2011] ‚ÄĚ Changing the slope of the curve corresponds
to increasing the demand own-price elasticity or the responsiveness of consumers with
respect to price changes ‚ÄĚ. The own-price elasticity (őĶnt) is introduced as a parameter
and tuned to adjust the new short-term demand response function (5.10) along with its
inverse (dual) function (5.11) to calculate the new price levels defined by parameters et
and fnt hour (Lagrangian multipliers).
This elastic demand function is designed to be implemented at all time steps and
for each node (‚ąÄt ‚ąąT, ‚ąÄn ‚ąąN).
According to Eq.(5.10) and Eq.(5.11) electricity
consumption levels will be adjusted in response to prices.
This is a communication
channel putting suppliers and consumers in close relation which is a great asset to
contribute to the system balance. Hence a real-time price (ŌÄnt) that is higher than the
anchor price (ŌÄ0
n) used as reference will results in a decrease in consumption for that
hour. Oppositely if ŌÄnt < ŌÄ0
n consumption will be driven up.
5.2.3.2
Integration of the elastic demand function into the model
Integrating short-term demand response that allows consumers to provide a degree of
flexibility to the system is achieved by replacing the fixed demand profile by hourly
elastic demand functions constructed as introduced in Subsection 5.2.3 (Elastic demand
function). This results in a different optimal solution at each hour of the time horizon
considered, leading to a new market equilibrium with new demand levels found while
optimizing the model that include demand flexibility. Adding this feature into the model
increases its degree of complexity since the optimal solution is calculated maximizing
the social welfare. The latter is the integral of the demand function minus the optimal
system cost (minimum) faced by producers while satisfying the demand.
Based on

The social welfare maximization problem (5.18) is expressed as the difference between
the consumer welfare, on the left hand side of the equation, and the system cost on the
right hand side of the equation. This is a modified version of the base case objective
function (4.1) defined as the system cost which can easily be identified between the
square brackets of the new objective function that includes demand flexibility.
The balance constraint at each node is modified accordingly since the demand profile
is changed, affecting the dual of this equation from which the marginal cost of electricity
are extracted for every time step:

==PAGE:88==
62
5 Model extensions
5.2.4
Data and assumptions
Demand data used are the same as in the base model. The entire demand is assumed to
be flexible with the same own-price elasticity (őĶnt) for every hours of the time horizon
considered.
The inflexible demand has been modelled using the same mathematical
formulation as the one that include an elastic demand function.
In order to reflect
properly this inflexibility, deviations (y+
nti and y-
nti) have been given boundaries equal
to 0. This will be used as reference for comparison with the different level of flexibility
implemented afterwards. Resulting demand levels after applying flexibility are used as
input for the base model (Chapter 4) to perform a second solve. This is needed to
extract the associated prices for these new demand levels since the dual of the balance
equation of the demand response formulation (5.20) does not stand for electricity prices
anymore.
Three different elasticity levels have been chosen according with previous
studies [De Jonghe et al. 2011], [M√ł ller, Erik, and Larsen 2006]: ‚ąí0.1, ‚ąí0.2, ‚ąí0.3. The
previously introduced stepwise integration method (Subsection 5.2.3 - Integration of the
elastic demand function) has been assigned steps sizes (U -
nti, U +
nti) for every node and
every time step to be 1% of the demand level (Dnt). Since 100 steps were created on
the left hand side of the anchor no load shedding is included in the model anymore (See
mathematical formulation in (5.18) and (5.20)). The creation of the elastic demand
function presented in Subsection 5.2.3 (Elastic demand function) requires a reference
price (ŌÄ0
n) being the weighted average price of electricity better known as fixed tariff.
The purpose of modelling DR being to check if there is flexibility already included
in the demand data used it is the historical fixed tariff of electricity that has been
used and referred as ‚ÄĚhistorical anchor‚ÄĚ.
In order to validate DR implementation
a sensitivity analysis on this reference price has been performed since the historical
anchor for 2013 (‚ČÉ38e/MWh
‚ąÄn) was relatively low compared with the mean price of
electricity calculated in the base model for the same year (calculated anchor). Therefore
calculations have been performed using the calculated anchor for 2013 (‚ČÉ55e/MWh
‚ąÄn)
for every node) as well as another value much higher (= 70e/MWh
‚ąÄn) in order to
assess the sensitivity of the model to this parameter.
5.2.5
Results
Results for the year 2013 based on historical and calculated anchor are hereafter shown
for the different own-price elasticity levels. These include for each case the calculated
day-ahead market prices (Figure 28, Figure 32), the demand variation per country
(Figure 29, Figure 33), the price duration curves (Figure 30, Figure 31, Figure 34,
Figure 35), RMSE values (Table 16, Table 19), R2 values (Table 17, Table 20) and
social welfare together with system costs (Table 18, Table 21). Scatter plots on the
calculated day-ahead market prices versus the historical value are available in Appendix
(Section A.6) together with the results of the analysis performed for a price anchor value
of 70e/MWh.

5.2.6
Discussion
Looking at the results for 2013 based on the historical anchor Figure 28 shows that
giving flexibility to the demand leads to lower and more stable prices for all countries.
The higher the own-price elasticity the lower the prices and the fewer the price spikes.
This ascertainment can be correlated to the different price duration curves (Figure 30
Figure 31) and is in line with previous studies such as [Rassenti, Smith, and Wilson
2002], [Andersen et al. 2006]. While the own-price elasticity increases the price profile
of each country is more flat with lower average value. This is mostly visible for Finland
and Sweden due to higher and more diversified prices before demand flexibility is applied
(for őĶ = 0). Figure 29 exposes the demand variation for each country for the different
level of flexibility. This demand variation is defined as the net difference between the
demand profile after demand flexibility has been applied (dnew
nt ) and the initial demand
(Dnt). Therefore a positive value indicates that the new demand level is higher than
initially meaning that the price of electricity (before including elasticity) was lower
than the reference price (ŌÄn0) used for the anchor resulting in an increase in electricity
consumption. Oppositely a negative value translate a decrease in electricity consumption
due to higher prices than the reference and this is what can be observed for all four
countries for the entire year in Figure 29. This does not look like DR since the demand
profile varies only in one direction decreasing the overall consumption. The too high
prices before including flexibility which are higher than the reference price are the
responsible.
Indeed it is known that the order of magnitude of the prices from the
==PAGE:99==
5.2 Demand response
73
base model are way too high compared with the historical value. Therefore the model is
trying to get as close as possible to the given anchor price by decreasing the electricity
demand accordingly to this price magnitude difference. This is confirmed by the RMSE
values (Table 16) that are performing better for every country as flexibility increases.
R2 are improving as well even though their true value remains unsatisfactory (Table 17).
Regarding social welfare and system cost analysing Table 18 results that the social
welfare is decreasing as the flexibility increases. This is the resulting of the assumption
that the entire demand is flexible. The demand curve is a line that is moving around
the anchor point with a decreasing slope (closer to horizontal) as the own-price elasticity
increases. Therefore consumers surplus decreases as their flexibility increases negatively
impacting on social welfare. However system cost is decreasing as expected since the
demand is reduced and less energy needs to be produced which is consistent with results
from [De Jonghe et al. 2011] and [Andersen et al. 2006].
Results for 2013 based on the calculated anchor are much more different with prices
that are less affected when calculations are based on the calculated mean price of
electricity (Figure 32).
Denmark and Finland are the only countries that show a
slightly different price distribution over the year. This is confirmed by the net demand
variation (Figure 33) were Denmark and Finland are the only two country that have
significant demand level adjustments. The values are not only negative anymore but
shared between demand increase and decrease depending on the hour of the year. The
poor price changes over the year can be explained by the fact that the calculated prices
are not very diversified and therefore closer to the calculated mean price of electricity
leading to a lower impact of the DR. Changes are mainly available for Denmark and
Finland because these are the country with the most unstable prices while Norway and
Sweden have prices already too close to the mean price of electricity used as a reference.
However significant load decrease are still noticeable at the beginning of the year from
January to March. Price duration curves displayed in Figure 34 and Figure 35 support
this analysis. No changes in the price distribution are observed for Norway and Sweden
regardless level of elasticity; tiny changes are observable for Denmark and Finland only
on the left side of the curves (high prices). RMSE, R2, SW and SC values respectively
shown in Table 19, Table 20 and Table 21 are logically not evolving much due to the
poor changes previously discussed.
The third analysis performed based on a higher anchor value of 70e/MWh completes
the sensitivity analysis on this key parameter of DR modelling.
Results shown in
Appendix Subsection A.6.3 confirm that DR is highly sensitive to the chosen reference
price. Demand is almost always increased because this price reference is most of the
time higher than the calculated prices for the four countries. Hence DR response tends
to move on the right hand side of the elastic demand curve increasing the electricity
consumption.
Scatter plots results available in Appendix Section A.6 are consistent with the previous
discussion of the effect that DR has on the calculated prices. Higher own-price elasticity
leads to less dispersed prices which can easily be visualized with more homogeneous
price clusters.
==PAGE:100==
74
5 Model extensions
Overall a different energy consumption pattern was observed when DR was introduced,
varying accordingly to the chosen price anchor around which the elastic demand function
was constructed as well as the magnitude of the own-price elasticity associated. The
magnitude of the changes in energy consumption were of the order of several hundreds
of MWh of electricity depending on the country and the time of the year considered
as well as of the own-price electricity of course. Assuming a middle case scenario for
change in demand (increase or decrease) of the order of 1000 MWh it would lead to
a change in consumption around 180 Watts per inhabitant in Denmark, Norway and
Finland. Whereas a change in consumption of about the half would be required per
Swedish citizen (population about twice as high as in the other three countries). This
seem to be a reasonable order of magnitude.
5.2.7
Conclusion
It has been shown that DR has been properly implemented into the model through
different levels of own-price elasticity applied to the demand. The higher the own-price
elasticity the more the demand is adjusted in respect with the reference price. The
sensitiveness of the model to this anchor price has been demonstrated through the
sensitivity analysis highlighting the importance of this parameter. Using historical mean
prices of electricity as an anchor has led to a general decrease in prices as expected
thereby flattening their distribution over the year which is consistent with previous
studies.
It has shown to improve the model accuracy but this should not be taken
for granted. Indeed DR might be present in the demand data used as input to the
model but not in such a proportion that high prices of the base model would scale down
to the expected prices. The model seeks to better represent the price level, diversity
and spikes in each country which is not achieved through DR modelling. DR however
scaled down the high prices presented in the base case which could be taken as a model
improvement at first but is not. Unsatisfactory results for DR are probably due to the
simple way it was modelled. Due to time limitation further research on DR could not
be conducted. Perhaps it should be included and investigated at a small scale (little
elasticity coefficient) and integrated to an enhanced version of the model that provides
better match of the historical prices (not as high as the one in the base model). This
could be interesting to be investigated through both own-price and cross-price elasticity
modelling with not the entire demand being flexible as it was studied in [De Jonghe,
Hobbs, and Belmans 2011] and [Taylor, Schwarz, and Cochell 2005] in order to simulate
future scenarios on demand-side management evolution. The willingness of consumers
to change and adapt their consumption is a hard thing to quantify, therefore such a
feature added in a more advanced model would be a useful tool to run scenario analysis
on the topic.
==PAGE:101==
5.3 Combined heat and power (CHP)
75
5.3
Combined heat and power (CHP)
5.3.1
Literature survey
The use of combined heat and power plants (CHP) is surely among the most efficient,
environmental-friendly and economical ways to produce electric power and heat. By
contrast to conventional thermal plants, cogeneration facilities avoid large discharge
of heat into the environment and use part of this thermal energy for e.g.
district
heating.
Higher efficiencies and smaller fuel consumption can therefore be achieved.
CHP plants are generally coupled with heat storage units to allow more operational
flexibility. Previous studies confirmed how the modelling of the CHP plants is necessary
while dealing with power systems based on high CHP installed capacity [Palsson and
Ravn 1994]. A study performed by [Zugno, Morales, and Madsen 2014] has been useful
in order to model the functioning of a combined heat and power plant. The optimization
of the joint production was implemented accordingly. The technical characteristics of the
power plants were analysed in [Weber 2004] where a complete description of the operation
modes of the CHP was available. The characteristics of the plants in Scandinavia were
assumed from previous projects of Ea Energy Analysis, Hofor and Dong Energy ([Ea
Energianalyse 2009], [Energi Denmark frozen policy 2014], [Hoved 2009], [Paulsen et al.
2014]). No stochasticity is assumed in the model. The heat and electricity demands
were considered as input parameters and no uncertainties were associated, which is in
discordance with other reports. In [Palsson and Ravn 1994] a stochastic programming
approach is used in order to solve the optimal management of a CHP plant along with an
heat storage, in [Zugno, Morales, and Madsen 2014] a robust optimization was used in
order to determine the optimal schedule of a CHP plant and in [Ravn and Rygaard 1994]
a similar problem was solved while using a stochastic dynamic programming approach.
Besides, differently from other methods proposed [Kristoffersen, Meibom, and Juul 2013],
no unit commitment was included for the power plants considered. These simplifications
were assumed with regards to the goal of this project. Indeed the aim of the thesis being
to represent big systems with simple models, no stochasticity and unit commitment were
included.
5.3.2
Description and expectations
One of the predetermined learning objectives of the thesis is to learn how to deal with
the joint production of the CHP plants in Scandinavia and the bidding of the outputs
in the related two markets (i.e.heat and electricity). The power plants considered in
the analysis are defined as Combined Heat and Power (CHP). The use of these units
is beneficial for the system since the utilization of the fuel energy is much higher than
producing electric power and district heat separately. This implies that the productions
of heat and power in the units are jointly constrained (See Figure 37). Therefore, the
optimisation of a system that includes these units will results in a multi-commodity
optimisation problem [Zugno, Morales, and Madsen 2014].
In this section the management problem for a couple heat-and-power system, which
==PAGE:102==
76
5 Model extensions
includes CHP plant and their interaction with the two markets, will be modelled. A
general description of the combined heat and power plants is available in [Horlock 1987],
for readers who are not familiar with the technology.
Figure 36: Scheme of the CHP system
Figure 36 shows the system considered for the analysis of the CHP plants. The core
of the system is based on the combined production of heat and power from the designed
power plants. The analysis is subsequently split in two areas, concerning the electricity
and the heat balance.
For electricity, the analysis will not be different from the base model. The electricity
demand will be satisfied by the power production from the power plants, wind turbines,
solar PV, import and export from the surrounding countries and power exchange within
the system. The power plants which are not classified as CHP (i.e.nuclear, hydro and
regulating plants) will also contribute to the production. The power produced by these
entities will be then ordered in the supply curve according to the merit order (i.e.
cheapest first), the day ahead electricity market will be cleared and the day ahead
market price will be determined.
On the other hand, for heat different steps are considered. The heat produced from
==PAGE:103==
5.3 Combined heat and power (CHP)
77
the CHP plants is evaluated in the first block, where the main priority is to satisfy the
heat demand. The surplus heat is then considered in the second block. Two possibilities
are available: heat can either be stored in a storage unit (where it can be re-used later on)
or it can be discharged into the environment (in case the heat storage associated is full).
The heat from the storage can be used to supply the demand when the CHP is producing
only power thus redistributing optimally over time the required heat production[Palsson
and Ravn 1994].
Two market clearing are implemented: heat and electricity. Each of them is based
on same mechanism but with different variables influencing the results. The results of
the base model showed how the day ahead market prices calculated were higher than
the historical. One of the reasons proposed to better match reality was to investigate
the joint production from CHP plants in the system and redistribute the cost among
the outputs. Thus the expectations for this section are to develop the understanding on
cost splitting between heat and power and investigate how the implementation of the
join production will influence the final marginal costs for the electricity and the heat.
5.3.3
Mathematical formulation and constraints description
Since the co-production of heat and power is interrelated and coupled by joint constraints,
these units represent the connection through the heat and power systems. Despite the
fact that the two outputs are sold in different markets and they belong to different
networks, the optimal management of the CHP units requires the joint optimisation
of the heat and power production [Zugno, Morales, and Madsen 2014]. Translated in
mathematical terms, the optimal production from the CHP units is solved through
a linear optimisation problem, with an economic objective (e.g.
cost minimisation)
subject to a set of operating constraints.
Due to the presence of dynamics in the
systems (e.g. fluctuation of the demand) hot water heat storages were implemented.
The model formulation is hereby presented, going through the mathematical model of
the production units, the one of the heat storage, and the joint optimization.
A. Production units
The heat and power output from a production unit g at the time t are indicated
as pg,t and qg,t respectively. The production of heat and power will be restricted
by the constraints imposed by the capacity installed.

B. Heat storage
The primary goal of the heat production is to satisfy the heat demand. For the
hours in which the production exceeds the demand, the heat is spilled.
Two
possibilities are then available: the waste of heat (through cooling) or the storage
in a heat tank.

by snt, cnt and wnt. The variables are indexed over the nodes, and not over the
generators. For a matter of simplicity it was assumed that one big heat storage
was available for each country. The dynamics of the storage are also considered

No constraints were fixed for the starting and ending values for the heat storage.

time.
C. Joint Optimisation Framework
The production units and heat storage described in the previous section have
to work together in order to guarantee the optimality of the system, since they
can both contribute to the satisfaction of the heat demand. The link between
production units, storage and demand is represented by the heat balance equation.
This ensure that, at every time, there is equilibrium between production, spill,
discharge and demand.

5.3.4
Data and assumptions
5.3.4.1
Power plants analysis
Due to the various range of power plants considered in the system, an analysis on the
different parameters was performed. The characteristics of the plants were observed
and the power plants were differentiated depending on their outputs.
The different
operations modes of the CHP were also investigated analysing the feasible regions of the
power plants.
‚ÄĘ CHP
The plants available in the database were investigated in order to define which of
them could be suitable for the heat and power production. After a careful analysis
of the type of power plants available in the system [GEO 2015], it was decided to
assume that all the power plant could be classified as CHP, besides the nuclear,
water based and regulating power plants.
The first for obvious reasons related
with the contamination of water/steam due to the use of uranium in the process1.
The second because most of the hydro power plants installed in the system are
correlated with the only electricity production. The third because this kind of
power plants (i.e. regulating) are used as ancillary service, therefore just for hours
in which it is required power regulation.
‚ÄĘ Power-to-heat ratio
The power-to-heat production ratio was defined according to the kind of power
plant. The parameter is useful for regulate the simultaneous production since the
different technical configurations allow different flexibilities while producing heat
and power. The power plants can be divided into three main groups depending
on the type of turbines and power plant layout: back-pressure, extraction and
1In theory, nothing prevents nuclear power plants to be cogeneration plants, and this has actually
been the case of several power plants in UK and in Sweden (and there hasn‚Äôt been any contamination
issue). However, there have been regulation/laws in Sweden to avoid that the district heat actually
derives from nuclear power plants, because of social acceptance issues, and also to ensure that the
district heating system would not face heavy modifications when the power plants would be shutdown
[Ericsson 2009]
==PAGE:107==
5.3 Combined heat and power (CHP)
81
condensing. For the back-pressure all the steam flowing out of the turbine is at
conditions close to the heat requirements, so usually above atmospheric pressure;
in the extraction plants part of the steam is extracted to allow more flexibility in
the heat and power production. In the condensing occurs only power production
and the plant produces only power. However, if used in co-generation-condensing
mode, the plants produce also heat. For the different kind, the relation for the
simultaneous production of p and q can vary. For the backpressure power plants,
the production of heat and electricity is fixed by the relation:
pel = ő≤qht

the power-to-heat ratio of the plant.

coefficient that relates the heat and power production through a linear relation.
(P max, Qmax) represents the extreme point of the line along with the production of
p and q can be varied. An example is presented on the left hand side of Figure 37.

5.3.4.2
Heat analysis
‚ÄĘ Losses:
Concerning the heat transfers, it is well known that in the reality there are losses
associated with the transmission and storage of heat.
Thus, parameters were
implemented accordingly in order to consider the losses:
‚Äď Heat demand: the heat demand was increased to compensate the losses
that occurs in the transmission of heat (e.g. losses in the piping and auxiliary
systems).
‚Äď Charge and discharge: the heat lost while charging and discharging the
storage was considered assigning different efficiencies for the fluxes of heat
going in and out from the storage
‚ÄĘ Heat profiles and heat demand:
As it is well known, the heat demand profiles for the country of Scandinavia
are somehow difficult to gather as public information. Data available present the
annual consumption by country [IEA 2015b] as well as forecast for the future years
[Werner and Constantinescu 2006]. However, in order to complete the task required
for the CHP plants, the hourly demand profiles for Denmark, Norway, Sweden and
Finland were required as input data for the model. With the data available (from
[IEA 2015b], [Werner and Constantinescu 2006] and [Balmorel 2015]) the profiles
were extrapolated and used for the analysis.
‚ÄĘ Methodology:
The hourly heat profiles, as well as the annual consumption, for Denmark East
and West were available. However, since the total annual heat consumption was
not coherent with the data published by IEA [IEA 2015b], the data were scaled
with the following proportion:
Hourlyold
Totalold
= Hourlynew
Totalnew
(5.39)
where:
‚Äď Hourlyold is the hourly heat profile calculated with the previous data
‚Äď Totalold is the annual heat consumption according to the database
‚Äď Hourlynew is the new calculated hourly heat profile
‚Äď Totalnew is the annual heat consumption according to IEA [IEA 2015b]
The heat profiles for Sweden, Finland and Norway were then calculated with the

same procedure.

Figure 43: P-Q diagram for the flexible and inflexible power plants
Figure 43 illustrates the power and heat production diagram (i.e. P-Q diagram) for
both the flexible and inflexible power plants. The PQ diagram for the inflexible power
plants (i.e. backpressure) is shown on the left hand side. For the units of this kind, the
power and heat production is bind by the power-to-heat ratio with a linear relation. The
P-Q diagram for the flexible power plants is illustrated on the right side. The production
can vary around a predefined area, with limits are imposed by the thermodynamic laws
as well as operating constraints, normally, as you would rather not operate a power
plant under a certain boiler load.
For some units (see green line on the right hand
image) the heat and electricity were produced following the upper bounds (see lines of
the polyhedron). Some other units (see the light blue area on the right hand image)
optimise the joint production of heat and power working in almost all the operating
modes allowed within the boundaries.

A sensitivity analysis was performed over the heat prices in order to understand how

==PAGE:118==

5 Model extensions
5.3.6
Discussion
The day ahead market prices calculated show great variability throughout the year with
seasonal changes. The prices are indeed slightly lower during the summer period. This is
the result of the interaction with the different kind of CHP plants. Indeed, the lower heat
demand in the summer (combined with the charge and discharge of the heat storage)
implies that the electricity production of each cogeneration plant can be increased at
the expense of the heat production, which corresponds to an upper left shift on the P-Q
diagrams. The comparison of the calculated prices with the historical values showed
that the model performs quite well. The analysis of the price durations curves highlights
that the values calculated follows the same development of the historical values. The
extremes are quite well represented and are matched exactly for almost all the countries.
The analysis of the scatter plots confirm the positive trend showed in the duration
curves. The correlation between the historical and calculated values is coherent with
the trend-line illustrated. Most of the calculated values match the historical. On the
other hand, some others are still scattered around, though not so far from the identity
line. This highlights how the model is still not representing perfectly the reality, but
it gets really close though. The RMSE and the coefficient of performances shows also
decent results. On the basis of the RMSE values, the implemented model is providing
good results. This is in line with the comparison with the base model. The values
presented for this extension are indeed higher than the base case investigated at the
beginning. The same for the values of the R2. The coefficient of determination reported
for the CHP is higher than the base case model for Sweden and Norway. The results for
Denmark and Finland are slightly lower. The outliers previously observed in the scatter
plots and price duration curves were identified as the responsible for the low values.
Figure 48 presents the correlation between demand and heat price. The results shows
that a lower heat demand implies lower heat prices. As soon as the demand increases,
more power plants need to be activated or the operation modes of the flexible CHP plants
needs to be adjusted. This results in a change in the marginal producing plant, most
often more expensive than the previous. The trend of the points confirms the behaviour.
However, just for some outliers, the correlation demand-heat price is not valid. These
are the hours in which the storage is satisfying the missing part of the demand.
In order to confirm the strong relation between the heat demand and the heat
prices, a sensitivity analysis was performed. The correction factor2 applied to the heat
demand was increased and decreased in order to investigate how the prices were changing
accordingly. The results illustrated in Figure 47 shows that an higher heat demand leads
to higher prices. On the other hand, lower heat demand implies lower heat prices. The
correlation between heat demand and heat prices was therefore confirmed and proved.
The investigation focused also in the electricity day ahead market prices while varying
the heat demand. The results of Table 23 illustrate how the electricity prices3 were also
2The correction factor was explained in Figure 5.3.4.2 and applied to the heat demand in order to
consider the share of the heat demand covered by the CHP plants.
3The prices presented are the mean price over the all year.
==PAGE:119==
5.3 Combined heat and power (CHP)
93
sensible to the variation of the heat demand, though with a different correlation. Indeed,
if the increase of the heat demand was resulting in an increase of the heat prices, the
opposite behaviour was observed for the average electricity prices. Thus higher heat
demand resulted in higher heat prices but lower electricity prices in average. On the
other hand lower heat demand resulted in lower heat prices but higher electricity prices
in average.

Correlated with the heat demand, the heat production was investigated focusing on
the two different kinds of power plants considered in the system. Figure 43 shows the
P-Q diagrams from both the flexible and inflexible power plants. The results are in line
with [Zugno, Morales, and Madsen 2014]. For the back-pressure plants (i.e. inflexible)
the production of heat and power follows a linear relation (left side image). This is
in accordance with the constraint implemented (5.27), where the relation between heat
==PAGE:120==
94
5 Model extensions
and power production was fixed by the power-to-heat ratio. On the other hand for the
condensing and extraction plants (i.e. flexible power plants) the P-Q relation is explained
by the area displayed in the right side of Figure 43. The boundaries for the area presented
were imposed by the heat and power capacity (5.24) and (5.23) respectively and by the
constraints imposed by the thermodynamics laws presented (5.25) and (5.26). In the
image empty and filled areas are identified. The empty areas represents the production
of power plants that operate at the limit of the boundaries. For these plants, the heat
and power production occurs only at the maximum limit allowed. On the other hand,
where the P-Q production is represented by a filled area the specific power plant is
working in all the possible operation modes.
Indeed, the filled area is composed by
all the points that represent the possible combination of heat and power production.
The relation highlighted in the figures and the considerations drawn prove that the
constraints implemented for the two kind of power plants are working effectively and
are in line with [Weber 2004].
The production of heat is then compared with the actual heat demand, in order
to investigate if the demand and the supply are in balance. The results illustrated in
Figure 44 show that for most of the hours of the year the match of the demand and the
production of heat is almost complete. This is identified by the dark blue line composed
by the points.
For the points out from the line, the relation demand-production is
different: points over the line represents hours in which the production is lower than
the demand. For the points below, the opposite. In these hours therefore, the storage is
helping on the demand satisfaction, charging and discharging accordingly.
Figure 46 shows the dynamics of the storage for these hours, focusing on the net
charge. The net charge is calculated as (charge-discharge) so the positive values represents
the hours of charge while the negative values represents hours of discharge. The opposite
happens for hours of over production. The dynamics of the storage were also analysed
in Figure 45 where it is shown the correlation between the surplus heat in the system
and the related spill4. The results are in line with [Schr√∂der 2011].
The analysis of the heat prices illustrate in Figure 42 shows the variability of the
heat prices during the year. The prices were presented in trend-lines, since more than
the actual values it was interesting to study their development in the different seasons.
The prices are higher during the cold seasons (i.e. periods of high demand), while they
are lower during the summer period (i.e. period of low heat demand). However, while
the trend-line for Denmark, Sweden and Norway followed the expectations, the prices
for Finland were 0 e/MWh for all the period simulated. The odd result was investigated
observing different parameters.
‚ÄĘ
Heat and power production
Table 24 and Table 25 illustrate the percentages of utilization of the two kind of
4The heat spilled will be then redirected to the storage
==PAGE:121==
5.3 Combined heat and power (CHP)
95
power plants. The former table refers to the heat production, while the latter to
the power production. The first table highlighted that for Denmark, Sweden and
Norway there is an higher utilization of the inflexible power plants. This result
seems in line with the low cost of the heat found for Norway and Denmark since the
heat produced with these plants comes as a byproduct and thus has zero marginal
cost associated. The share of utilization of the flexible power plant is also in line
with the results presented in Figure 42. The higher use of flexible plants results
in higher heat prices. Sweden has the highest heat prices, and this is correlated
with a 33% usage of the flexible plants where the heat is produced with a cost
associated.
The other countries follows accordingly.
However, for Finland the
results appear to be odd. Indeed, the use of the flexible plants is the highest in the
system (66%). According to the previous consideration, Finland should therefore
present the highest heat prices. Despite this, the resulting marginal costs for the
heat are zero. The analysis of the power production highlighted the same result.
Indeed, the results of Table 25 shows that Finland presents the highest use of the
flexible power plants.
The results therefore shows that the zero heat price for Finland is definitely an
unexpected result. What it is expected indeed is that the heat prices for Finland
should be the most expensive.

‚ÄĘ
Power plants installed in the system
The capacity installed for both the heat and the power was investigated. Table 26
and Table 27 show the results. Finland presents a slightly higher share of non-flexible
power plants in the system, compared with the other countries. On the other hand,
concerning the power installed, the node presents more flexible plants. Nothing
out of the normal was spotted from this data, but again confirms that the energy
==PAGE:122==
96
5 Model extensions
mix of Finland is composed by both flexible and inflexible plants.
‚ÄĘ
Sensitivity analysis on the heat demand
A sensitivity analysis was performed on the heat demand.
The values were
increased and decreased up to 20 % in order to investigate how the heat and
electricity prices were reacting. For Denmark, Sweden and Norway all the results
satisfied the expectations and behaved like they should. Furthermore a correlation
heat demand-heat/electricity prices was identified. However, for Finland nothing
changed while varying the demand. It seems that the node was insensitive to the
modifications of the system.
‚ÄĘ
Generator phasing out
As the system was designed, the marginal cost of heat is associated with the
kind of power plant producing. If the heat is produced by the non-flexible power
plants, it is considered as a byproduct. On the other hand, the heat produced
with the flexible plants have a cost associated and its production could vary along
the feasible region. One of the reasons for which the heat prices for Finland were
null for all the year could have been that the production of the non-flexible power
plants was satisfying completely the demand. However, the results of Table 24
prove it wrong. Another sensitivity was performed, phasing out all the inflexible
generators from Finland. It was investigated which would have been the price of
the heat if all the flexible generators were phased of of Finland. A simulation was
performed. However with the time horizon considered (i.e. one year) the problem
was unfeasible, for obvious problems of missing capacity. The time of the analysis
was restricted to a week. Once the non flexible generators were phased out, the
flexible generator were taking over the heat production, with incredibly high prices
though (around 200 e/MWh). Due to the fact that it was not possible to run the
simulation for one year, no results are presented.
The analysis performed over the odd result just confirmed its queerness. All the
results presented highlighted that the heat prices for Finland should not be zero, but
they should be among the highest in the system. And even if the analysis of the results
showed Finland was behaving differently comparing the the rest of the countries in the
system, no ‚Äôresponsible‚Äô was identified for the zero heat prices.
Beside the odd results, the implementation of the joint production of heat and power
enhanced the performances of the system under study compared to the base case. The
calculated day ahead electricity prices showed in average values close to the reality. Just
the upper extremes were not well represented. Indeed, these are prices determined in
extraordinary situation. The spikes for Denmark and and Finland were due to a mix of
events (congestion of the lines, tripping of units, ... ) that are difficult to model. Due
to the modelling hypothesis assumed in the creation of the base model, these situations
were not likely to happen.
As it is, the implemented model also considers that all the producers bids in the
market at their real marginal costs, with no strategic behaviours or gaming. Therefore,
==PAGE:123==
5.3 Combined heat and power (CHP)
97
from the modelling point of view, it is difficult to forecast and calculated the price spikes
that occurred in the reality. The inclusion of the heat in the analysis surely changed
the way the power plants were producing energy. Indeed, being the heat demand also
a constraint to satisfy, the plants had to optimize the joint production at the cheapest
price. The relatively low day ahead market prices that result from the calculation reflects
this behaviour.
5.3.7
Conclusion
The joint production of heat and power was implemented for the system under study.
The model was validated through the analysis of the heat results, which all confirmed
that the constrains implemented were working as expected and in line with [Zugno,
Morales, and Madsen 2014]. A strong correlation was identified between the heat demand
and the heat prices. The sensitivity analysis conducted over the head demand confirmed
and proved the relation. The heat prices showed a seasonal variation due to the change
in the demand. The heat prices for Finland had a constant value equal to 0 e/MWh
through all the year.
An analysis was performed in order to understand the cause,
however no ‚Äôresponsible‚Äô was identified for the odd result. The electricity day-ahead
prices showed a big variability on an hourly scale. The comparison with the historical
day ahead market prices showed a good correlation. Furthermore, the analysis suggested
that the electricity prices are strictly correlated with the heat demand. Indeed, a positive
variation of the heat demand would lower the prices. And the opposite for the other way
around. The joint production of heat and electricity highlighted therefore the strong
bond between the parameters considered. The implementation of the CHP therefore
enhanced the performances of the model and allowed to obtain good results. The model
extension based on the CHP will be therefore considered as main component for the new
model that will be implemented.
==PAGE:124==
98
5 Model extensions
5.4
Hydro power modeling
From the base case results the modeling of the hydro-power plants was identified as
one of the most important driver in the day ahead market price determination [Baldini,
Zeitouni, and Richert 2015]. Indeed, due to the great amount of capacity installed in the
system (almost 60%[IEA 2015a]) and due to its low marginal cost associated, day-ahead
market prices were directly proportional to the amount of energy produced from the
hydro-power technology. For these reasons, particular focus has been put on a better
modeling of this technology. Hydro units were modelled in different ways in order to
find the best representation of the hydro-power production and try to match the reality.
Two main ideas were considered:
‚ÄĘ Package of energy: the capacity of the hydro-power plants is limited by a
capacity factor of 0.455 over the time horizon considered. This is done in order
to constraint and limit the hydro-power production. Indeed, an extensive use of
these plant would dry out their reservoir leading to an impossibility of additional
electricity production for next time period.
‚ÄĘ Inflow and the reservoir level: the hydro-power production will be modelled
according to the method proposed by [Skjelbred 2011], considering the inflow and
the reservoir level of each dam. References were also made to [Dueholm and Ravn
2004]. The power production and the maximum reservoir level will then be limited
in order to reflect the reality.
The first method was used in the base model previously presented in Chapter 4. Due
to the unusual results, a deeper investigation was performed in order to enhance the
performances of the model. The second method exposed above represents a change in the
way the hydro was modelled. The two methods will be presented in the following sections.
After, the results of the simulation will be displayed and the relative considerations will
be drawn.
Hydro power production as a package of energy
As first trial for the hydro-power
modelling, a non-dynamic behaviour of the hydro-power plants was introduced. In this
configuration the production of power was directly associated to the capacity, without
considering any other technical characteristics (e.g. height of water columns, inflows,
turbine efficiency, ...). It was assumed that the each hydro unit could produce power with
the only limit being the installed capacity. However, due to the fact that an extensive
use of the whole capacity would have lead to a dry-out of the reservoir (reservoir not
implemented in this model but anyway considered in the analysis), the capacity available
for each plant was constrained through a capacity factor specific to the time horizon
considered. This allowed an utilization of each hydro plant for which the level of water
(at the end of the use) would not be lower than the beginning. Results for this method
are the same reported in the base model.
Indeed the hydro power production as a
package of energy was the set-up selected for the base model simulations. (For all the
considerations and results see Section 4.5)
==PAGE:125==
5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
99
5.5
Hydro power scheduling with inflows and
reservoirs
5.5.1
Literature survey
With the results of the previous section in mind, a new way to model hydro-power
technology is proposed. Five different models will be investigated based on the hydro
modeling proposed by [Skjelbred 2011] with a similar method that was also used in
[Dueholm and Ravn 2004]. Different constraints and limitations will be implemented,
in order to discover how the day-ahead market prices are influenced.
The different
models aim to match the reality and find a good compromise between modelling and real
operations. The five models investigated are:Base hydro model, Hydro model constrained,
Hydro model with hourly deviations, Hydro model with weekly deviations and Hydro
model with penalties. The first two models are based on the deterministic hydrothermal
scheduling problem proposed by [Skjelbred 2011]. A strategic analysis of seasonal inflow
producing water values as guidelines for reservoir optimal utilization was proposed. The
terms water value is presented in different papers([Dueholm and Ravn 2004] and [Stage
and Larsson 1961]) and represents the cost of alternative production while determining
the supply function for the hydro production. The aim of these two models is to provide
a good representation of the hourly supply function for electricity production from
hydro-power plants with large reservoirs.
The third and fourth model aim at reflecting the reality as close as possible. The
historical data gathered from Nord Pool [NordPool Market 2015] were used as main
input for the analysis. An investigation was performed on how the model would deviate
from these values, with a cost associated to the deviations.
The last model was implemented considering the technical and environmental limit
imposed by the reservoir level. Indeed, reservoirs base hydro plants are typically operated
with the goal of maximizing their revenues, while meeting other legal water requirement
[Fayad, Peralta, and Forghani 2012] [Jager and Smith 2007]. Upper and lower bounds
will be implemented in order to study the reservoirs levels behaviour during the year.
5.5.2
Description and expectations
The main idea is to consider each singular dam and analyse how the input (i.e. the
inflow), the existing level of water (i.e. reservoir level) and the output (i.e. the outgoing
flow of water that will be converted in electricity) are correlated. Different constraints
will be implemented in each model, in order to study various configurations of the
hydro-plants. The expectations from the results are to find a different configuration for
the hydro-power plants that enhances the performances of the simulation.
==PAGE:126==
100
5 Model extensions
5.5.3
Data and assumptions
The hypothesis assumed for the five models are here presented and described. The data
treatment will be also presented along with the explanation of the parameters assumed
for the different analyses.
5.5.3.1
Type of hydro plants
All hydro-units were considered to be ‚Äôdam-based‚Äô, neglecting the fact that two different
kind of plants (i.e. run on river and dam-based) exists in the reality which is the biggest
assumption made for the proposed models. This is considered because of two reasons
related to the data available: inflow and power plants characteristics.
First, concerning the inflow, the information available from Nord Pool were limited
[Nordpool 2015]. The data were provided as an overall weekly inflow for all countries,
without considering the split between run-on-river and dam plants. Thus, for the sake
of simplicity all plants were assumed to be ‚Äôdam-based‚Äô. Second, the characteristics of
each power plant were missing and the best provided was an overall installed capacity
installed per country, without any distinction on the kind (e.g. ror and dam-based),
position, head and capacity. Since the modelling of both run on river and dam-based
would have implied different assumptions and complications for the model, hydro units
were assumed to be flow-regulated. Similar issues were found by [Dueholm and Ravn
2004] while dealing with hydro technologies, regulated and un-regulated flows. Through
the literature survey it was not possible to find any article that clearly specify the
percentage of run-on-river and dam-based plants in Scandinavia.
In case the hydro power modelling would have been split considering both kind of
configuration, the implementation should be done as follows. The dam based plants
would have been implemented exactly as it has been done, with a power production that
varies over time in function of the demand and of the water available in the reservoirs.
On the other hand, the run on river plants would have required a different attention.
These kind of plants are directly installed on the river and are designed to provide a
constant amount of power.
Therefore the hydro power production would have been
split in two parts: un-regulated and regulated flow. The first is associated with the
run-on-river technology while the second with the dam-based. The constraint (5.41)
that would have consider the total hydro-power production (phydro
g,t
) as the sum of the
un-regulated (pror
g,t) and regulated (pdam
g,t ) production reads:
phydro
g,t
= pror
g,t + pdam
g,t
(5.41)
5.5.3.2
Analysis of the Inflow
Additional information should be given about the data treatment since the inflow is one
of the most important input parameters. Considering the geographical configuration
of the Nordic countries, there are two main contributors to the reservoir inflow, rivers
and the snow-melting.
The first is expected to provide a constant amount of water
==PAGE:127==
5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
101
throughout the year while the second is expected to present a daily variation related to
the sun hours and a greater contribution during spring and summer. The two inflow
profiles were calculated consequently and are shown in Figure 49 on an yearly scale. A
weekly zoom is also illustrated in Figure 50 for better understanding where it is highlight
the main difference between river and glacial inflow. Indeed while the former present
constant values throughout the week, the latter shows some daily variations with peaks
corresponding to the most sunny hours of the day.

5 Model extensions
The raw data available from Nord Pool were adapted to be used in the database.
Data available on a weekly resolution in GWh needed to be hourly transposed and
converted into MWh.
For the river inflow, the following steps were followed:
1. The data were converted from [GWh] to [MWh]
2. The data were adapted to an hourly profile distributing the weekly value available
over the hours of the week. The hourly inflow was calculated as:
Inflowhourly = Inflowweekly
Weekhours
(5.42)
where the Inflowweekly are the raw data and weekhours are the hours in a week
(i.e. 168).
3. The hourly values were scaled to the number of hydro power plant of each country
Since the raw data were provided for 52 weeks (that corresponds to 8736 hours/year)
while the hours for 2012 and 2013 were 8760 and 8784 respectively some values were
missing. These filled with the values of the following year (i.e. the next chronological
coming week).
Concerning the glacial flow, the same procedure as for the river inflow was adopted
with a slight difference in the calculation. The steps are as follows:
1. The data were converted from GWh to MWh
2. The data were adapted to an hourly profile distributing the week value available
over the hours of the week. However, the snow melting is usually associated to
specific sunny daily hours. Thus a correlation was implemented in order to consider
this. Since the greatest melting would occur during the peak hours of sun, a sin
function was implemented in order to create a profile with daily variation (see
Figure 50). A correction factor őī was implement in order to consider this variation.
Inflowhourly = Inflowweekly
1 + sin(t)őī
Weekhours
(5.43)
where:
‚ÄĘ Inflowweekly are the raw data
‚ÄĘ weekhours are the hours in a week (i.e. 168)
‚ÄĘ t are the hours in chronological order (i.e. 1, 2, 3,...)
‚ÄĘ őī is the correction factor
3. The hourly values were scaled down to the number of hydro-power plant of each
country
The so called correction factor was added to vary the profile of the glacial inflow.
The greater őī the greater the difference between the maximum and minimum value for
the glacial profile (i.e. amplitude of the sin function). This was considered to assess the
day-ahead prices sensitivity to different inflow profile given. A sensitivity analysis was
performed on the correction factor in order to confirm this. The results are shown in
Subsection 5.5.9.
==PAGE:129==
5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
103
5.5.4
Base hydro model
5.5.4.1
Constraint description
The previously introduced hydro power constraint (4.8) was substituted with a new
mathematical formulation.
rg,t = rg,t‚ąí1 ‚ąípg,t + Ig,t
‚ąÄt ‚ąąT, ‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro
(5.44)
where rg,t represents the level of the reservoir g at the time t, rg,t‚ąí1 the level of the
reservoir g at the time step before, Ig,t the Inflow in the reservoir g at the time t and pg,t
the power generation of unit g at time t (5.44). The constraint will enable the power
production to be directly correlated with the inflow and the reservoir level of the unit
considered. Though, boundaries were applied for the variables listed in order to limit
the level of the reservoir and the power production. A small remark has to be made
before getting to the results. Indeed the production of power from the hydro-technology
should have considered some other parameters. An additional equations should have
been added, beside constraint (5.44), in order to consider the technical characteristics
of the hydro power plants. The ‚Äômissing‚Äô line is hereby presented (5.45).
pg,t = ő∑gbg,thtotg
‚ąÄt ‚ąąT, ‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro
(5.45)
where the bg,t should be replaced in (5.44) instead of pg,t. The power production
should therefore depend on the efficiency of the plant(ő∑g), on the flow of water in the
turbine (bg,t), the height of the water column (htot) and the gravity acceleration. However,
due to the fact that the added equation would have lead the model to be non-linear, it was
decided to avoid this additional constraint. Despite of the choice, an investigation was
performed in order to understand which parameters were necessary to implement in the
model in case the previously described constraint (5.45) was considered. The complete
analysis is reported in the Appendix in Subsection A.3.4 where the suitable values to
use for the analysis were investigated using of clustering techniques, dendrograms and
centroids.
5.5.4.2

Day Ahead Prices
Figure 51 presents the calculated day-ahead prices for 2012 and 2013. While prices
for Finland are oscillating between 35-80 e/MWh, for Sweden Norway and Denmark
the prices reaches minimum values of 0 e/MWh for most hours of the year. No big
spikes can be spotted, beside one for Denmark around January with a value of almost
100 e/MWh.

A quick look on the scatter plots suggests that prices are distributed in various price

flattens the production as well as the prices with the same generators more often being

are close to the reality, since the RMSE‚Äôs are among the lowest (19.41e/MWh and

5.5.4.3
Discussion
Figure 51 shows the calculated day-ahead market prices.
Prices for Denmark and
Finland seems more realistic following an almost linear pattern throughout the years.
Norway and Sweden presents different results due to the fact that hydro-power is the
main technology in their energy mix. Hydro modelling has a greater influence on the
resulting prices which can explain the continuous ups and downs observed. In Figure 94
and Figure 95 the calculated day-ahead market prices are compared to the historical
values through price duration curves. Calculated values match more for Denmark and
Finland than for Sweden and Norway, for the same reasons previously exposed about
the difference in energy mix for these two pairs of country. The results for 2012 and 2013
did not differ too much. Nevertheless 2013 presented a slightly better fit. An analysis on
the scatter plots in Figure 96 and Figure 55 concludes the same. The linear fit was more
representative for Denmark and Finland for both years. The values are indeed scattered
around the identity line and follows the linear pattern. On the other hand, the prices
calculated for Norway and Sweden tends to be gathered around singular values showing
the low variability associated to the large share of hydro power installed in these two
country having a flattering effect on day-ahead prices formation.
From both Table 28 and Table 29 it can be concluded that Denmark and Finland are
the country in which the calculated prices fitted the most. Finland was found to be the
best fit with the historical data of 2012 according to the RMSE values of 18.23 e/MWh
and coefficient of determination of 0.343. Then follows Denmark with RMSE of 19.41
e/MWh and an R2 of 0.260. Norway and Sweden presents the lowest values. Overall
comparing results to the base case, the model performs better with this new configuration
of hydro-power modelling. Nevertheless, there is still room for improvements.
==PAGE:134==
108
5 Model extensions
5.5.4.4
Conclusions
Results for the base hydro model were analysed and commented. From the price duration
curves analysis it was concluded that the model performed the best for Denmark and
Finland for the year 2013. The calculated prices for Sweden and Norway were influenced
by the way hydro-power technology was modelled due to the large installed capacity in
these countries resulting in a bigger deviation from the historical values. The analysis
conducted on scatter plots revealed confirmed better results for Denmark and Finland,
while both Norway and Sweden presented different groups of prices that did not fit
perfectly with the identity line. The considerations that followed the analysis of the
RMSE and R2 highlighted how the outliers influenced the final results. Even if these
indicator show better results compared to the base model, the low values of the coefficient
of determination pointed out how the the model accuracy is still weak suggesting that
improvement should still be made.
Therefore the base model implemented will be
enhanced to achieve better results.
==PAGE:135==
5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
109
5.5.5
Hydro model constrained
The previous implemented model was modified expecting that results would improve
results meaning, more realistic day-ahead prices and a closer representation of the
hydro-power plants operations. The enhancement were:
‚ÄĘ Reservoir level maintain: an analysis on Figure 52 suggested that the reservoir
levels were dragged to zero at the end of the simulation. Due to this unrealistic
scenario, the values of the reservoir levels were fixed to end at the same as at the
start of the simulation. This was done analysing the development of the reservoirs
in Norway (available at [Statnett 2015]) for a period of 20 years.
‚ÄĘ Reservoir level beginning value: Since the reservoir levels were implemented
as free variables in the system, the choice of starting level was determined by the
model itself. In order to give a glimpse of reality the starting level was fixed for all
reservoirs according to the historical values (available at [NordPoolSpot 2014]).
‚ÄĘ Hydro spilling: it represents a variable introduced to compensate the physical
limits imposed by the reservoir levels. Since in certain hours of the year the amount
of water available could exceed the limit (i.e water already in the reservoirs +
inflows), it was needed to allow the system to spill water. Power plants are allowed
to let the water flow through the turbine (or other side connections) without any
power production associated in case of overflow.
5.5.5.1
Constraint description
The two additional constraints previously described were implemented in the model as:
rt[start],g =rt[end],g
‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro
(5.46)
rt[start],g =Hg
‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro
(5.47)
The expected level of the reservoir at the end of the year is imposed to be the same as
at the beginning (5.46). Beside, the starting point of the reservoir level for all the dams
has to be equal to the collected historical values (Hg) (5.47). These new constraints
were implemented in the model and simulations performed from which the results are
shown in the following Section.

highest price is 100 e/MWh, while the lowest around 48 e/MWh, both for Denmark.
The prices showed a general stability throughout all the year for both 2012 and 2013.

Reservoir levels
Figure 57 exhibits the calculated reservoirs levels. The reservoirs level follows the same
pattern and development as the historical value reported by [Statnett 2015], TSO of
Norway. Therefore, the level is decreasing during the winter, when the rivers are frozen
and the glaciers are not melting. During the summer the snow melting and the greater
rivers flows fills the reservoirs.
Indeed, starting from the summer/spring period the
reservoirs are filled again.
Price durations curves
Price duration curves for all four countries are presented in Figure 58(b).
The
calculated data are compared with the historical, in order to report the difference
between the two. The results are presented for 2013; the results for 2012 are available
in the appendix.
The results suggested that the implementation of the new hydro
model affects the results positively. Indeed, when ordered in price duration curve, the
calculated day ahead prices shows less variability than it was in the previous model. The
extremes begin to be more noticeable. The same for the plateau. Indeed, even though
it seems completely flat, a careful observation of the values will show that the line is
not straight but it has a curvy shape. Since the values of the calculated prices are very
similar the difference cannot be observed. For an easier understanding of this issue, the
reader can analyse the scatter plot in Figure 59 and the related description.
