The resulting prices on the scatter plots show that the model allows the calculation
of diversified marginal producers. Indeed, no more price groups can be identified with
only one or two main clusters. Furthermore values are similar to each other, without
great deviations (besides some rare outlier). This is the reason for the almost-flat slope
of the plateau in the price duration curves. The prices are still more distributed around
the centroids of each cluster, rather than around the identity line. The values of the
RMSE and coefficient of determination confirm this trend. For the RMSE the lowest
value is identified for Finland in 2013 with 21.12 e/MWh. Furthermore, the R2 are
found to be very small with the lowest values in Norway.
==PAGE:140==
114
5 Model extensions
5.5.5.3
Discussion
Figure 56 shows the newly calculated day-ahead market prices and suggest that the
implementation of few additional constraints impacted the results. Prices seem to be
more diversified and some spikes appear. An average price higher than the historical
can be noticed with: 56 e/MWh (calculated) versus 35 e/MWh (historical). Sweden is
the country were prices are the most stable through the year, the same for Norway with
some additional spikes. Oppositely, Finland and Denmark present a higher variability
on prices. The reason for these diverging results lies in the difference in energy mix of
both country pairs. Denmark and Finland rely more on fluctuating technologies (PV
and wind power) rather than secure and controllable plants (e.g. hydro power) as it
is the case for Norway and Sweden. The calculated reservoirs level profile (Figure 57)
have great similarity with the historical values of [Statnett 2015]. The reservoirs are
filled over spring and summer when the inflows are copious due to the snow and ice
melting. They are then discharged during the winter when the needs of electricity are
higher but the inflow not so abundant.
An analysis of the price duration curves in
Figure 58(a) and Figure 58(b) suggests that with the new way of modelling hydro, the
extremes are better represented. Even though the slope of the price curve is not as
steep as the historical and the magnitude slightly too high, the extremes begin to be
more consistent with the reality. Beside the fact that the calculated price curve began
to take shape, values are still considered to be too high compared to the reality with all
the calculated prices over the historical curve. Observing the scatter plots in Figure 59
the low slope for the calculated price duration curve can be explained. The correlation
between the two sets is following the linear pattern identified by the identity line. The
prices are gathered around one or two epicentres with values scattered around will a
small difference between each other. This highlight that the same technology is almost
always the marginal producer. The analysis of the root mean squared error and the
coefficient of determination confirm how the calculated values are still far to represent
the reality. Overall, even though the shape of the price duration curves have shown some
interesting improvement as well as the scatter plots, this configuration of hydro-power
modelling has worse results than the base case when looking at the RMSE and R2.
5.5.5.4
Conclusions
New constraints were added to the basic hydro-power model expecting to better represent
the reality.
Simulations were performed in order to assess whether or not the new
constraints were enhancing the model. Results analysis showed an improvement in the
price duration curve representation, specifically on the extremes.
The scatter plots
provided an explanation for the low-slope of the plateau highlighting that the prices
are various but still too concentrated around a singular epicenter.
Furthermore a
general trend of higher calculated values compared to the historical was noticed. RMSE
and the R2 indicators surprisingly pointed out that this supposely enhanced version of
hydro-power modelling was worse than the base case.
==PAGE:141==
5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
115
5.5.6
Hydro model with hourly deviation
With the following third and fourth model introduced after in Subsection 5.5.7, we tried
to be as close as possible to the reality with the values of the reservoirs level. Since
the historical values were available, it was decided to use the data in order to push the
model to follow the same path. The idea is that some boundaries will be given to the
rgt variable (while before it was almost free) in order to match the real path. For this
model specifically, the hourly profile of the reservoir level is implemented.
5.5.6.1
Constraint description
The historical values were extrapolated from [NordPool Market 2015] and adapted
accordingly to the previously described methodology. To push the variable to follow
the historical path, a price penalty was associated each time the value of the variable
was deviating from the historical one. The price assigned is proportional to the deviation.
The following new constraint was implemented as,
rgt = Ehour

==PAGE:146==
120
5 Model extensions
5.5.6.3
Discussion
Figure 60 reports the day-ahead market prices.
The analysis of the graph suggests
that this hydro implementation drove the prices to incredibly high values reaching
the threshold of the load shedding.
For 2012 and 2013 the load shedding occurs in
correspondence to times in which the reservoir level is very low. On the other hand,
during the summer period the prices show a stable development. The correlation between
the reservoir level and the resulting day-ahead prices is identified and proved.
An
analysis of on reservoirs levels shown in Figure 61 highlights that the shape of the curve
is different compared to the historical values. The up and down regulations, illustrated
in Figure 62, highlighted the fact that the variable implemented in the model (rgt) did
not follow the historical values as closely as expected. Consequently, the price duration
curves in Figure 63 reflect the bad behaviour of the model: the calculated curves are way
off compared to the historical values. The scatter plots, the coefficient of determination
and the root mean squared error simply support results inaccuracy.
RMSE values
are very high and R2 very low compared to the base model, reflecting that this way
of modelling hydro is performing badly. The scatter plots finally showed how the fit
between the calculated and the historical values is far from being linear. Few values are
lying in the path of the identity line, while all the others are scattered around.
5.5.6.4
Conclusions
The hydro model implemented was verified through the different evaluation methods
used for every feature study and validation. All of them showed that the model is not
reflecting the reality and is not performing well. The calculated day-ahead market prices
showed that load shedding occurs during the year. Besides, the up and down regulations
highlighted how the limit imposed (i.e. the hourly hydro profile) is not consistent with
the dynamics of the model. The RMSE, R2 and the scatter plots confirms that the
calculated values are far off from the historical, leading to a worse scenario than the base
case. Given these consideration, this hydro model was concluded to be bad performing.
The duty to follow an hourly profile did not provide the expected results. Thus, more
improvements should be done in order to continue with the same idea. Therefore the
following Section investigate if and how the results will change when more freedom is
given to these deviations.
==PAGE:147==
5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
121
5.5.7
Hydro model with weekly deviations
Similarly to the previous model, the constraint implemented will try to impose the rgt
variable to fit the reality. However in this configuration the weekly profile previously
calculated will be imposed for the reservoir level. This means that the model will be
constrained to match the historical weekly value for every period of 168 hours (= 7days
x 24 hours). Doing so the constraint from the previous model will be ‚Äôrelaxed‚Äô and more
freedom will be given to the model:
5.5.7.1
Constraint description
The constraint implemented is not different from the one implemented in the previous
extension (5.48), if not for the time horizon. In this model indeed, the weekly profile
will be considered as follows:
rgt = Eweek
gt
+ r+
gt ‚ąír-
gt
‚ąÄt ‚ąąT, ‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro
(5.51)
r+
gt, r-
gt ‚Č•0
‚ąÄt ‚ąąT, ‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro
(5.52)
where Eweek
gt
denotes the historical values that identifies the filling of each dam g at time
t on a weekly scale. All the considerations for the deviation variables, costs correlated
and modification of the objective function are the same as the previous model.
5.5.7.2
Results
Day Ahead Prices
Figure 65 show the calculated day-ahead prices for 2012 and 2013.
The prices are
varying through the years with a seasonal pattern. In winter period prices are among
the highest reaching peaks of almost 100 e/MWh (Denmark), while in summer they are
lower with minimum value around 35 e/MWh.

by the same calculated marginal cost) are getting smaller and more prices are found
all along the identity line. This, even for Norway and Sweden, the two countries where
the hydro power capacity installed was negatively affecting price development for the
previous models.

5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
125
5.5.7.3
Discussion
This model showed improvement compared to the previous configurations. As expected,
the additional flexibility introduced allowed simulations to perform better.
The day
ahead prices presented in Figure 65 shows great variability with few spikes during the
year. The range of the calculated prices is still a bit higher than the historical values
though. The analysis of the price duration curves in Figure 69 suggested the same. The
blue line representing the calculated values is slightly higher than the black line, that
identifies the historical values. The slopes of the curves are similar, besides the steps in
the blue curve. The reasons of the steps were found to lie in the sudden change in the
marginal generator producer, that implied a different (and most often higher) marginal
cost associated. The scatter plots drawn in Figure 105 and Figure 70 helps to understand
the reasons behind the steps in the price duration curves. Indeed, the calculated prices
are gathered in groups. That means for different prices in the historical values set, the
calculated prices are the same. The ‚Äôlines‚Äô of prices are therefore the responsible for the
steps in the price duration curves: the longer the lines, the longer the steps. The fit
between the values follows the linear pattern of the identity line. Besides some extreme
points (outliers), the calculated values were in a good range. Overall, Table 34 shows and
support good result accuracy of the implemented methods. The values of the RMSE
and R2 confirmed the validity of the model and the better performances compared to
the base case. Interesting observations can be made through the calculated reservoirs
levels in Figure 66 and the up and down deviations in Figure 67. It seems that the
model calculates reservoir levels that are different from the real values. Nevertheless,
the response of the calculated prices to these deviations led to positive results. Indeed,
the calculated day-ahead market prices with the model under study showed the best
performances among all the so far tried models. The reservoir level was found to be
again the main driver on price formation.
5.5.7.4
Conclusions
A weekly deviations model was implemented, in order to boost the ‚Äôhydro with deviations‚Äô
model previously implemented by giving it more freedom. The calculated day-ahead
market prices showed a great variability throughout the year.
The analysis of the
price duration curves showed that the calculated prices follows the same pattern as
the historical values, though with slightly higher values and some steps in the curve.
The reasons for the steps were explained analysing the scatter plots. The prices were
gathered in various groups, with an almost liner correlation between the calculated and
the historical values. The results of the RMSE and the R2 showed that the model
performed good (since the results were better than the base case) and that the current
model could be considered the best among all the implemented before.
==PAGE:152==
126
5 Model extensions
5.5.8
Hydro model with penalties
In this last hydro model the idea is focus on the development of the reservoir level
over the year and create ‚Äôdanger regions‚Äô in order to constraint the variable. Figure 71
displays a graphical illustration of the constraints implemented. The upper and lower
bound created will force the model to limit the rgt variable between the two extremes.
These two zones where selected for specific reasons. The upper danger zone represent
the maximum limit allowed for the reservoir level. If the limits is exceeded, there might
be problems related to water storage in the basin. Even though the hydrospill variable
would allow the by-pass of water through the turbines without power production, this
situations is likely to be avoided. Indeed, this would represent a ‚Äôwaste‚Äô of energy that
could be used to generate additional revenues if a proper management of the reservoir
level is done. For the same reason, the lower bound imposed is useful in order to maintain
a certain level of water in the reservoirs. If the level would go below the limit imposed,
there would be issues in the re-filling of the basins and the power plant would need to
be stopped in order to refill its reservoir. Thus the two bounds imposed represents the
upper and lower limits for the reservoir guarantying the safety in the availability of water
in the basin.

5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
131
5.5.8.3
Discussion
The day-ahead market prices illustrated in Figure 72 show some weakness for this
configuration of hydro . The fact that the load shedding occurs in almost half of the
hours of the year highlight how the limit imposed for the model are too strict. It seemed
that the ‚Äôdanger region‚Äô implemented along with the penalties associated were too tight.
Figure 73 presents the values of the variable associated with the reservoir level. The
constraint implement posed a strict limit on the development of the reservoir levels
variable thus conditioning negatively the entire model. The price duration curves and
the scatter analysed in Figure 75 and Figure 76 simply reflect the bad behaviours of
the model. Even though the right hand of the calculated price curves seems to follow
the pattern of the historical values, on the left hand the prices are completely off. The
analysis on the scatter plots concluded the same.
Indeed a range of prices at 2500
e/MWh (i.e. load shed prices) are identified as the outliers from the identity line. The
high values of the RMSE confirms the considerations previously described: the model
implemented does not perform very good. The same can be drawn from the coefficients
of determination.
5.5.8.4
Conclusions
The discussions section highlighted that the constraint implemented was too strict. The
results of the simulations showed that the load shedding occurred in most of the hours
of the year resulting in prices that were off compared to the historical values.
The
reservoir level limit imposed was identified as the responsible. Being too constrained,
the model shed the load, rather than pay the penalties associated with the up and down
deviations. Therefore, it can be concluded that even though the theoretical formulation
of the constraint was promising, the results were not as good as expected. A further
investigation on this model could try to improve the constraint implemented, treating
the input data in a even more accurate way.
5.5.9
Sensitivity analysis on the glacial inflow
As it has been previously described in Subsection 5.5.2 (Analysis of the Inflow), the
inflow was considered as input data for the models. However, the inflow assumed for the
simulations was the river-based, where a fixed amount of water was available throughout
the week, according to the historical data. Nevertheless, different kind of inflows were
considered, in order to represent at best the reality. The second option proposed was
the glacial inflow, where the amount of water available was varying during the 24 hours
periods according to the melting of the snow/glaciers. Simulations were performed in
order to investigate whether the glacial inflow would have been more suitable as an
input for the system. The analysis were performed over the different corrections factors
adopted. The assessment was done considering the final average prices, the root mean
squared error and the coefficient of determination. The year 2012 was considered as
test-year.

Table 38 present the outcome of the sensitivity analysis. The results suggests that the
change from river-based inflow to glacial-inflow does not really bring to any particular
changes to the model. The values of the RMSE slightly vary in a range of ¬Ī 0.02
e/MWh. The same behaviour is identified for the coefficient of determination where
the values are changing in a range of ¬Ī 0.004 compared to the river-inflow case. The
average prices shows the same behavior. Thus, it can be concluded that the results
do not change significantly while choosing the inflow to be river or glacial based. The
river-based inflow was then considered for all the analysis. However, the idea of the
glacial inflow combined with the river inflow could be part of future works. Indeed,
given the different range of configuration of the inflows in the Scandinavian panorama,
some other investigations can be made on this parameter and study how (and if) different
kind of inflows can twist the results of the implemented model.
==PAGE:159==
5.5 Hydro power scheduling with inflows and reservoirs
133
5.5.10
General discussion on the different hydro models
implemented
The analysis of five different methods highlighted how the hydro-power modelling is
one of the main driver of the day-ahead prices formation in the Scandinavian system
under study. This was concluded after obtaining different result for every hydro model
proposed. The calculated prices showed to be highly sensitive to the changes implemented.
From the first to the second method, prices showed great differences while simply
constraining the reservoir levels. The same on the deviations methods implemented,
where the calculated prices changed while implementing an hourly and a weekly constraint
on the level of the basins. Accordingly, the same happened for the last model assigning
penalties to some specific danger zones. The reservoir level was identified as the common
driving factor of the observed changes for all the models implemented. Indeed, whichever
modifications on the levels or operation modes of the basins, the resulting prices were
changing.
The reservoir level should be pictured as a big battery.
The potential
available in this ‚Äôbattery‚Äô has to be used considering the availability of water and the
electricity demand in the market. The fact that the power production from the hydro
technology come at almost zero cost, it allows the owners of the hydro power plants
to adopt strategic behaviours and game with the market. Every small modifications
in the reservoir operations will lead to a big change in the day-ahead market prices.
As it was stated at the beginning of the chapter, the hydro capacity installed in the
systems represents almost 60% of the total. Thus, the necessity to model the hydro
power technology is fundamental in order to understand and properly represent the
development of the day-ahead market prices in the Nord Pool. Besides, the hydro power
technology tent to flatten the prices throughout the year. On the other hand, the analysis
of the historical day-ahead prices showed that (despite the great hydro capacity installed
in the system) there are fluctuations over the year. Therefore, from the the hydro power
chapter it can be concluded that the prices available in Nord Pool are not only driven
by the technology limits (considering also the availability of the primary fuel) but they
are the results of technology limits and strategic bids. That means (in this specific case)
that even though hydro power plants owners could bid in the electricity market at low
marginal costs, they will not. The decision of the bids will depends on the opportunity
costs. Since the hydro power plants are the most flexible in the systems, their production
could be varied instantaneously whenever there would be a need or a wish for it. For
the same reasons, producers will ‚Äôhold‚Äô the power production for moments of the year in
which their water would have higher values and to generate higher profit. Therefore it
can be concluded that to properly reflect the day-ahead market prices of the Nord Pool,
modelling of the hydro-technology is crucial but not enough. The strategic behaviour
of the hydro-power plants owners should also be included in the modelling in order to
reproduce the opportunity cost bidding strategy.
==PAGE:160==
134
5 Model extensions
5.5.11
Conclusions on the hydro models
An analysis on the hydro power technologies was carried out in this chapter.
Five
different models were implemented and analyzed aiming at finding the best model that
would issue day-ahead market prices as close as possible to the reality. The analysis
showed that one model among all performed the best: Hydro model with week deviations.
However, the results showed that the calculated prices were still not matching the
historical values.
The reasons for the gap were identified in the modelling of both
technical and behavioural particulars. The hypothesis assumed for the modelling and
the simple Scandinavian system implemented were the main reasons for the ‚Äôfailure‚Äô
on the technical part.
Concerning the behaviour of the hydro power plants owners,
the discussion highlighted how the bids in the electricity market were based more on
the opportunity costs, rather than the real marginal costs of production. Therefore,
future works to improve the models presented could include a better modelling of the
technologies and the behaviours of the power plants owners when submitting bids. The
state of art is still far to be reached, but the work performed can be a useful benchmark
for future studies.
==PAGE:161==
CHAPTER 6
Joint model
6.1
Base model enhanced
The base model used as starting point for this research has been further investigated
through several features that were believed to be relevant to achieve better results: unit
commitment, demand response, combined heat and power and hydro modelling. The
later were separately added to the base model and their influence on results accuracy
observed to determine whether or not the extension has enhanced the model. The idea
was to consider the best extensions and combine them together in one joint model to
propose the best combination of model extension that would lead to better accuracy of
results.
The implementation of the unit commitment led to observe the dynamics of the
power plants production. Units were properly responding to their assigned constraints
and the expected behaviour on the production profile of the different technologies could
be observed (ramping up/down, start-up etc...). The simulation time was identified as a
limiting factor due to the inter-temporal constraints that UC implies. The exponential
increase in simulation time along with the larger time horizon considered did not allow
a simulation for an entire year. Therefore even though the unit commitment seemed to
be an interesting feature to add in the model it was decided to not include it into the
joint model. This decision is supported by the fact that Nord Pool prices are defined
by the TSO‚Äôs (market clearing) that do not consider UC - [Martin, M√ľller, and Pokutta
2012]
The demand response was implemented in order to investigate whether or not some
flexibility was already included in the demand data used as an input. Price-responsiveness
of consumers have been investigated as well to check the change in demand levels together
with the resulting day ahead market prices.
Indeed demand responses scales prices
(accordingly to the chosen anchor) and has a flattering effect on prices distribution
reducing the number of spikes which goes against the reflection of extreme prices that
is a weakness of the base model desired to be improved. The scaling effect on prices
of DR has been discussed not to be the correct way to achieve better results because
other extension were believed to be responsible and more promising for the same purpose
(CHP‚Äôs). Hence DR was not considered as a relevant feature for the final joint model.
The hydro-power modelling is the extensions where most analysis were made through
five different cases investigated. Results showed how the hydro with weekly deviations
model performed the best among the others. Since hydro-power modeling was identified
of paramount importance when modelling the Scandinavian power system it was withheld
==PAGE:162==
136
6 Joint model
as an extension part of the proposed joint model.
Last but not least modelling of the co-production of heat and power of the CHPP‚Äôs
was found to improve significantly the performances of the simulations and the related
results. Therefore modeling of the combined heat and power plants was also selected as
an extension in the final model.
With these considerations the base model was modified accordingly to perform a
final simulation. No mathematical formulation nor data assumptions are displayed for
this model since it is a combination of the explanation provided in the model extension
in Chapter 5.
6.2
Results
The results presented are only the one relevant for the price analysis of the year 2013
excepted for the RMSE and R2 values shown for both years. Complementary results for
2013 as well as the ones for 2012 contributing to the model validation are available in
Appendix Section A.5. Results of this Chapter will be illustrated but not discussed since
they are part of the final discussion of the present study (Chapter 7) where a comparison
between the joint model and the base model is addressed.

the system were around 80 e/MWh and 12 e/MWh respectively (Denmark). Highest
prices occurs in the beginning and end of the year (winter) while the lowest (besides
some spikes in December) occurs in the middle period of the year.

CHAPTER 7
General discussion
This Chapter gives the final discussion on the proposed joint model that summarizes the
different topic investigated throughout the project. Results are discussed and compared
to the base case model in order to assess the degree of improvement.
The calculated day-ahead prices for 2013 shown in Figure 77 are different from the
ones calculated in the base model (See Figure 9). First the diversity of prices is much
better in the final model compared to the base with more fluctuations over the year.
Besides the resulting prices reflects more what can be expected. A seasonal variation
is observed with prices high in the winter, intermediate during spring/fall and low over
the summer. The prices magnitude of the base model were found and argued to be too
high. The newly calculated values for the joint model are much lower oscillating around
40 e/MWh versus 55 e/MWh for the base model. The combination of the enhanced
hydro modelling and CHP plants lead to better results.
The previous findings are consistent with the price duration curves gathered in
Figure 78. Comparing the latest with the price curves originally issued (See Figure 12)
enhancement in term of price magnitude as well as price diversity can be observed. The
calculated values are almost perfectly matching the plateau of historical prices. This
highlights the scaling effect towards lower prices that CHP plants modelling has been
found to be responsible for. The plateau that was perfectly flat in the base model results
is slightly tilted as expected and desired for the joint model. This shows more diversified
prices over the year with a larger set of generators that are the marginal producer over
the time horizon considered. Better extremes prices for all the countries were calculated
but still far from the historical values. For Sweden e.g. the highest and lowest price
calculated are 60 e/MWh and 20 e/MWh while in the reality there are 120 e/MWh
and 0 e/MWh respectively. The other countries shows similar differences in order of
magnitude. Price spikes associated to extraordinary events are not well reflected because
of the lack of modelling on the transmission side, zones splitting as well as the eventual
outages that could occur in the system. Lower and negative prices are also badly reflected
due to the fact that stochasticity is not considered (of the wind for instance).
Scattered plots results allow a better visualization of the difference between the
calculated and the expected prices to be matched and are compared to the base model
results for each country in Figure 14.
Prices for Denmark in Figure 79 show very
satisfactory results with all the prices gathered around the identity line that represent
the perfect correlation between the two data sets. A slight difference can still be noticed
in the price magnitude and very extreme prices are not matched. However these results
on the scattered plot are acceptable and much better than the base case model ones.
==PAGE:172==
146
7 General discussion
For Sweden the values are gathered in Figure 80. The average magnitude is consistent
with historical prices but with many hours during which prices are still too high or too
low. It does not seem that prices are generally too low or too high since the cluster is
spread above and below the identity line. Extreme prices are of smaller value than the
ones observed for Denmark for instance but still not correctly represented. Price groups
can be identified inside the cluster (i.e vertical lines) which shows that the diversity of
the generators setting the price over the year still can be improved. The same data
gathering is shown for Norway in Figure 81 and similar analysis as for Sweden can be
concluded. The magnitude is satisfactory with some possible improvement on diversity
and extreme prices reflection. To finish on the scattered plots the regression for Finland
is displayed in Figure 82. The cluster of values is also concentrated around the identity
line which is a good result. However price groups can clearly be identified for Finland as
well as a higher concentration of points on the left hand side. This suggests that slightly
too low prices are calculated for Finland via the joint model. The same phenomena was
observed in the base case results but in much worse proportion. The joint model has
improved on these but not enough apparently so there is still room for improvement.
Overall on the scattered plot it can be concluded that the joint model performed better
for all four countries. Prices groups are still noticeable but are much more diversified
compared to the base case results. The representation of the extreme prices can still be
improved and a slight improvement on price magnitude is also desired.
RMSE and R2 values are respectively reported in Table 39 and Table 40 for the joint
model while base case model values are reminded in Table 3 and Table 4. The joint
model did better for 2013 and 2012 compared to the base model when looking at the
RMSE‚Äôs. Norway and Sweden report the lowest values with 7 and 9 e/MWh respectively
for 2013 which is a big improvement compared to the associated base results values of
18.5 and 19 e/MWh.
Similar improvements on RMSE‚Äôs for 2012 can be observed.
Denmark and Finland have also reached better values but with smaller proportion of
enhancement. Focusing and correlating the RMSE values to the R2 outcome shows an
inconsistency for the joint model. Indeed higher RMSE values for 2012 are associated
the best coefficient of determination while the most satisfactory RMSE values observed
for 2013 have a poor R2. This strange result can be explained by the fact that year 2013
had more production from renewable energy sources as shown in Figure 8 leading to
higher prices fluctuations over the year. In Figure 7 the outliers corresponding to very
extreme prices for Denmark can easily be spotted. These two observation can explain
this inconsistency regarding RMSE and R2 since the former is a absolute measure of fit
while the latter is a relative measure of fit. Price being more homogeneous in 2012 the
associated coefficient of determination takes better values than for 2013 where prices are
more scattered and diversified. A positive error (calculated price too high) can somehow
compensate for a negative error (calculated price too low) if prices are more gathered
which is the suspected effect observed on the R2/RMSE inconsistency. However these
indicators have as well concluded on a better fit of the joint model compared to the base.
The joint model is considered as the mix of the CHP and hydro with weekly deviations
extensions. Thus, the most important parameters were also investigated. Concerning
==PAGE:173==
7 General discussion
147
the hydro-power section the focus is on the reservoir level and on the deviations from
the historical values. Figure 84 shows the level of the reservoirs considered in the model.
The results obtained are in line with the previous of the hydro extensions and no big
changes were observed. The reservoirs were filled during the summer period because of
the associated melting of the glaciers and the inflows of the rivers. They reached the
peak around fall, and then decrease gradually during the winter time, when the use of
the hydro-is more intense and the inflow is reduced. The path followed during the year
is almost similar to the one reported in Stattnet. Though this proved that the hydro
part of the joint model was working correctly.
The positive trend is also confirmed
by Figure 85 that presents the calculated deviations from the historical values. The
trend followed by the calculated values is similar to the results obtained in the singular
hydro model. However, the order of magnitude of the deviation is way smaller. Indeed
for the year 2013, the up deviations are almost zero from June to November. For the
other values, the order of magnitude is decreased of 20%. This shows again the big
improvements obtained with the joint model. Despite the improvements observed in the
deviations, the values are still not zero, as it would be desired. Indeed null deviations
would imply that the model is working exactly as the reality. The model can thus still
be improved.
The analysis performed on the heat part confirmed what was concluded in the CHP
Section, but with improvements. The implementation of the CHP already showed better
quality of results, confirmed by the analysis of the joint model. The relation between
the heat demand and the heat production was found to be linear for most of the times.
Outliers, representing periods of charge and discharge of the heat storage, were also
present. The correlation between the heat demand, the heat prices and the electricity
prices was confirmed with greater heat demand resulting in higher heat prices but lower
electricity prices. The analysis of the P-Q diagrams highlighted and explained the better
performances of the joint model. Indeed, Figure 86 shows that the flexible power plants
worked in more operating modes throughout the year. This is identified observing the
areas: there are more points and more filled regions. This means that the flexible plants
adjusted their production according to the demands (heat and electricity) for most of
the hours of the year, more than what they used to do in the CHP extension proposed.
The combination of the hydro and the CHP blocks led to greater utilizatiron factor of
these plants. The results of this combination enhanced the quality of the resulting prices,
both for the electricity and the heat. For the inflexible power plants, the same linear
relation was observed again confirming the validity of the constraints imposed.
==PAGE:174==
148
==PAGE:175==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusion
8.1
General conclusion
In this study the main concerns of building a modelling tool aiming to reflect the
reality of Nord Pool day-ahead prices formation have been investigated. A base case
model was implemented in Python¬ģ-Gurobi¬ģ (See Section A.1 for the description of
the experience) and further investigation conducted on different features considered
promising for improvement. The first calculated day-ahead prices from the reference
model were diverging from the reality in term of both magnitude and distribution over
the time horizon considered. Price diversity was found to be very low because of small
groups of units being the marginal producers for most of the hours of the year. This low
quality of results obtained has led to a deeper focus on four key aspects: (I) hydro-power
modelling, (II) combined heat and power plants modelling, (III) unit commitment, and
(IV) flexibility of demand.
From the five different hydro model investigated the Hydro model with weekly deviation
has shown to perform the best among the others. Better variability of prices has been
achieved as well as improvement on the magnitude of these.
A higher diversity of
generators setting the price throughout the year has been found. These enhancements
on calculated prices were consistent with the different indicators used to assess whether
a feature was contributing to results accuracy.
The Root Mean Square Error and
coefficient of determination have reflected and validated the improvement brought to
the model by this better way of modelling hydro-power plants. Even though the weekly
deviation set-up was proven to be the best tested model, limitation on the modelling
of both technical and behavioural particulars have been discussed. These limitation
are in line with more advanced models dealing with the same issue when scheduling
hydro power over the year [Meibom et al. 2003]. ‚ÄôGaming‚Äô through bidding strategies
of hydro-power plants is suspected to be part of the challenge when modelling such
technology as well as advanced technical properties that should be considered. These
are later on addressed in the discussion of future work.
Modelling of Combined Heat and Power plants was performed pursuing the idea that
its absence in the base model was responsible for the high order of magnitude of the first
calculated day-ahead prices. CHP plants implementation was validated by concluding
on the strong correlation between heat demand and heat prices in term of magnitude as
well as for the seasonal variation observed. Besides, the resulting P-Q diagrams proved
the validity of the constraints implemented and the correct functioning of the power
plants‚Äô operating modes. The bond with electricity was highlighted as well concluding
==PAGE:176==
150
8 Conclusion
that the heat demand was directly impacting on the day-ahead prices. Indeed the higher
the heat demand lower the electricity prices observed. This is in line with the expectation
at the start of this project believing that CHP plants modelling would have an impact
on the average price of electricity over the year.
The starting expectations concerning Unit Commitment have evolved throughout
the project. The modelling and better understanding of a Mixed Integer Program was
part of the beginning learning objectives of this thesis has been reached.
However
the complexity and challenges associated to this particular system modelling feature
have been discovered and understood as the project evolved.
Linear pricing for the
MILP of non-convex and non-continuous nature that UC modelling involves was one
of the main challenge faced but understood.
Limited analysis were performed due
to the computational time limitation associated to the inter-temporal constraints of
the MILP. Nevertheless UC was successfully implemented and power plants dynamic
production were observed consistent with their assigned technical constraints.
This
feature contributed more to the academic acquisition of knowledge than to model accuracy
enhancement.
It was also concluded that the contribution of UC would not be the
best topic to further investigate when aiming at matching the reality of the Nord Pool
day-ahead prices development since it is not considered in the real market clearing
algorithm used by the Scandinavian TSO‚Äôs - [Martin, M√ľller, and Pokutta 2012].
The last task was flexibility of demand through modelling of Demand Response
putting the change in electricity usage by end-use consumer from their normal consumption
pattern in response to change in the price of electricity, under the scope. Due to time
constraints only a limited investigation was conducted on the topic via the implementation
of a simple elastic demand function using own-price elasticity coefficients. Positive effects
from DR on system cost and day-ahead prices were observed with higher own-price
elasticities decreasing the system cost.
Benefits on the social welfare could not be
observed due to the simple modelling adopted with the entire demand considered to be
flexible. However future advanced modelling ideas have been proposed such as modelling
only part of the demand to be flexible and add cross-price elasticities for instance. We
also believe that DR should be considered in a model that provides more accurate results
than the one proposed and that does not only include the day-ahead market. In that
case DR will better find its place to study scenarios on future demand changes related
to political orientation chosen by the different countries.
The implementation and analysis of all the key features has brought significant
knowledge on the topic of energy system modelling. Better insight on Mixed Integer
Programming, Python programming, market mechanisms and behaviour of the different
components of such model have been acquired throughout this master thesis. The final
result is consistent with the initial objective of building from the beginning a market
model that, to some reasonable extend, reflects the reality of Nord Pool. This simplified
model of the Nord Pool is in line with the beginning goal of creating a simple model
that represent a big system able to draw reasonable, quick results. Understanding of
the mechanisms driving the price formation on the day-ahead were necessary to build
==PAGE:177==
8.1 General conclusion
151
such model. These were studied and identified through the extensive literature survey
performed on the relevant topics. Extreme prices on an yearly price duration curve could
not be properly reflected. Nevertheless this result should not be seen as a limitation since
other more advanced models present the same [Meibom et al. 2003], [Balmorel 2015]. The
final result is an open source market model1, representing the Nord Pool reality, giving
reasonable results and that is meant to be further developed by the suggestion provided
in the future work.
The created tool can be useful in different ways and for different actors that compose
the energy system landscape of the Nord Pool. E.g. governments, to implement new
energy scenarios and study their impact on the system from a general perspective. It
can also provide quick and reliable answers on price formation which would be useful
for energy intensive consumers or public authorities for instance. Study of technicalities
such as interconnection development or congestion (if the network is better modelled:
zonal split for instance) could be used by the TSO‚Äôs. To finish with power producers and
investors could use such a tool to simulate the relevance of building a new power plant
of a given technology type or to assess whether or not it is worth it to decommission an
old power plant.
1Available at: https://github.com/ORION-DTU/Scandinavian-Power-Market-Modelling.git
==PAGE:178==
152
8 Conclusion
8.2
Future works
The results and discussion showed that the joint model performed better than the
base case model implemented.
The model extensions proposed improved the model
accuracy and showed satisfactory output results.
However the reality was still not
completely matched.
Nevertheless most of the models investigated in the literature
survey faced similar limitations, some of which have been addressed by our own work.
The ‚Äôweak points‚Äô of the model were identified while comparing the hypothesis assumed
with previous studies on the same topic. Suggestions for future works are hereby listed.
‚ÄĘ Stochasticity of the input parameters: In this project the input parameters
(e.g. wind and sun production, heat demand, electricity demand) were assumed as
known data. Other studies [Palsson and Ravn 1994], [Zugno, Morales, and Madsen
2014], [Weber 2004], [Dueholm and Ravn 2004], [Morales et al. 2014] highlighted
the importance of considering stochasticity when modeling power systems having
a large share of renewable energy sources. Uncertainties are a reality that need to
be considered since they often lead to a mismatch between demand and production
affecting electricity prices.
‚ÄĘ Balancing market: the balancing market is naturally related to the previously
introduced stochasticity. Indeed the power traded in the balancing market is a
result of the mismatch between supply and demand. Thus the implementation of
the balancing market in the model proposed would allow to investigate not only
the day-ahead market but also the further layers of the market (i.e. hours right
before the delivery energy).
‚ÄĘ Power Transfer Distribution Factors (PTDF‚Äôs) The PTDF‚Äôs relate to the
power flow between countries modelled as nodes.
This feature will bring the
model to be flow based raising the need to investigate how the grid reacts to
different power injection at each bus/node of the network [Duthaler and Emery
2008]. PTDF‚Äôs are expected to improve results since they are used in reality by
the different TSO‚Äôs of the Nord Pool. A first attempt to model PTDF‚Äôs has not
provided satisfactory results and is available in the prior work on which this study
is based [Baldini, Zeitouni, and Richert 2015].
‚ÄĘ Zone splitting: The four countries modelled as the main nodes were assumed
to be a single zone behaving as a copper plate.
Under this assumption power
flow and eventual line congestion within a node were disregarded. The same for
the heat. Zone splitting at each node could lead to better results and represent
more accurately the reality [Fennelly et al. 2013]. Adding the AC transmission
lines inside the nodes, local heat storages combined with the CHP plants and the
location within a node of each unit would improve the validity of the simulations,
perhaps correcting the model limitations in terms of extreme price representations.
==PAGE:179==
8.2 Future works
153
‚ÄĘ CHP strategic bidding: The co-production of heat and power was implemented
according to the technicalities of these plants. Thus the producers were bidding
in the market purely according with the marginal cost of production. However
in the reality the bidding of the power and heat in the two markets is based
on the opportunity cost rather than the simple marginal cost [Schaumburg-M√ľller
2006]. The implementation of strategic behaviour for the bids in the market should
provide different results. Furthermore it will give a glimpse of reality considering
the gaming that characterise the heat and power market.
‚ÄĘ Run-on-river and dam-based plants: Hydro-power plants were all assumed
to be dam-based for simplification purposes. Furthermore an approximation was
made on the power generation neglecting the technical characteristics (such as
head, efficiency, height of water column) of each power plant thus not reflecting the
correlation between the inflow in the turbine and the power production. A better
modeling of the hydro based power plants will surely represent better the reality
and increase model accuracy - [Kristoffersen, Meibom, and Juul 2013], [Dueholm
and Ravn 2004]. Furthermore a split differentiating the run-on-river plants from
the dam-based ones would be interesting to investigate.
‚ÄĘ Phasing out of power plants: Denmark aim at begin fossil fuel free by 2020
[Ministry of Climate Energy and Building 2012]. Thus the total installed capacity
of the power plants using fossil fuels will be reduced accordingly to the goal. The
future energy plans of Sweden, Finland and Norway will also imply modification
to the energy mix in the countries. Both Sweden and Finland for example are
currently discussing the choice of phase out or maintain the nuclear reactors [Maja
Fjaestad and Petri Hakkarainen 2012]. Norway introduced several years ago a
CO2-tax on hydrocarbon fuels, and that has resulted in a shift in the use of
energy in refineries and offshore platforms, and thus has impacted the oil and gas
prices [Barde 2004]. Future developer of the project should investigate whether
the system (e.g. for Denmark) could withstand the fossil fuel based power plant
phasing out in term of installed capacity and how the system should be reinforced
in order to undergo such changes. Beside the model could also be improved to
include the impact of environmental policies/political actions.
‚ÄĘ Import and export: Import and export were included in the model as enforced
packages of energy and no price was associated to them. Thus enhancement of
the model should consider a better modelling of the import and export. Possibly
representing the dynamics of the power exchange with the neighbouring countries.
The outcome should present a more realistic scenario.

APPENDIX A
The appendix
A.1
From GAMS to Python
A.1.1
Introduction
The art of modelling energy systems has risen in the past few years due to the greater
integration of the different electricity generation technologies in the energy systems. The
latest introduction of uncontrollable electricity production technologies (i.e. renewable
energy sources) in the energy systems arise the need of some tools to control the balance
between demand and production.
These tools were found to be software where the
production technologies, the demand and the entire interconnected energy system were
modelled. Simulations were then performed in order to forecast future developments in
the system considered. One particular feature was considered to be important while
running the simulations.
This is the optimization.
In mathematics and computer
science, an optimization problem is ‚ÄĚthe problem of finding the best solution from all
feasible solutions‚ÄĚ[Kann 1992]. Given the fact that the energy resources available are
running out and the renewable energy course are intermittent, an optimization problem
on demand-supply is what is currently required. Various tools can be used in order
to create models that reflect the reality as close as possible. The most well-known for
energy analysis purpose can be listed among Gams¬ģ, Python¬ģ, Matlab¬ģ, R¬ģ, Mosek¬ģ
and many other more. For the analyses performed for the presented project two main
software have been used: GAMS¬ģ[Gams 2015] and Python¬ģ[Python 2014]. The former
has been used for the beginning stages of the project. Subsequently, the model was
moved to Python. Various packages were added to the software for the data treatment.
The reasons for the change of software will be explained, as well as the new advantages
of Python. Considerations will be added at the end of the chapter, in order to explain
the experience with the toll selected and provide recommendations for future utilisation.
A.1.2
From GAMS¬ģ to Python¬ģ
In this chapter the motivations for the transposition of the original model from GAMS¬ģ
to Python¬ģ will be presented. The reasons for the change of tool will be presented, along
with the perks and pitfalls of both the software.
The optimisation was the main goal of the ORION model and of the research
question. The parameters to optimize, in the specific case, are a set of power plants and
interconnection systems in order to satisfy the electricity demand with the least possible
costs. In order to complete the tasks, it was necessary to use tools that provided the
==PAGE:190==
164
A The appendix
possibility to optimize. The GAMS¬ģ software was the first tool selected. The main
reasons behind the choice were the following:
‚ÄĘ Academic tool
‚ÄĘ Freely available licence
‚ÄĘ Experts available
GAMS was among the tools learned during the two years of master. Indeed, the
software was previously used in one of the compulsory course of the Master of Science
in Sustainable Energy (Modelling and Analysis of Sustainable Energy Systems [DTU
2015]). During the course we were taught about the fundamentals, the functions and
the uses of the software. Therefore we decided to use the tool for the analysis since it
was already know. Due to the fact that the software was going to be used for research
purposes and anyway inside the university environment, the licence was available and
free of charge. Since the software is well used inside the DTU environment, there was
the possibility to ask tips and suggestions from the relevant experts on the software.
Therefore, some problems were solved asking to the relevant teachers and learning how
to use the software in a good way.
However, some of the qualities presented can also be counted as disadvantages. The
fact that the licence is distributed for free to the users just for research in the university
environment and the expensive costs related with the purchase discouraged any further
use of the tools.
This because one of the main idea of the project was to let the
code/model to be open source, in a way that future researchers/works could use it.
Besides, the GAMS¬ģ software does not have a big community on the net, where problems
can be posted and solved. Besides the relevant people that knew about the software, it
was difficult to find any other help.
Therefore, being the master thesis a work that supposed to be carried out entirely
from the student, with tools and ideas that might be used also out from the academic
environment, it was decided to change the programming language and Python was
selected. Perks and pitfalls for Python¬ģ (as well as for GAMS¬ģ) are reported in Table 41.
The main advantages of Python¬ģ are to be open source and to have a great active
community in the net. Therefore difficulties, troubles and doubts were usually solved
browsing on the relevant pages since a lot of people might have encountered already the
same problems. Besides, the intuitive way to write equations and constraints and the
user-friendly interface helped on the modelling.
==PAGE:191==
A.1 From GAMS to Python
165
Table 41: Advantages and disadvantages of GAMS and Python
GAMS
Python
Advantages
‚ÄĘ Software taught in university
‚ÄĘ Open source software

‚ÄĘ Easy data handling
‚ÄĘ Complementary libraries
Disadvantages

‚ÄĘ Few experts available in the academic environment
‚ÄĘ Not intuitive programming language
‚ÄĘ Not well used software
‚ÄĘ User-unfriendly interface
Different libraries has been used in order to boost Python¬ģ and have better performances.
Each of the extensions has been useful for simplify the execution of the optimization
problem. Two main library should be named among all: Pandas¬ģ and Gurobi¬ģ. Pandas¬ģ
is the data analysis library and it allows an easy handling of the data. Due to the fact
that big databases were used in the optimisation processes, it was necessary to have a
tool that would allow an easy use of big amount of data. This was successfully done
with Pandas¬ģ, allowing an easier use (compared to GAMS¬ģ) of the input data required.
On the other hand, Gurobi¬ģ was the selected solver for the optimisation processes in
Python. As it was reported from the website, Gurobi¬ģ is the state-of-the-art mathematical
programming solver[Gurobi 2015]. The solver is available without charge just for academic
purposes. However, despite the fact that the solver used for the optimisation needs to
be purchased, other solvers could be used for the same process. Among these, the most
famous are: APM Python[APM 2015], CVXOPT[CVXOPT 2015], Pyomo[Pyomo] and
pyOpt[PyOpt 2015]. The complete list of optimisation solvers available for Python can
be found at [Python 2015].
A.1.3
Considerations and suggestions
The programming language used for the project allowed an easier approach to the
problems to solve. Despite some beginning difficulties related with the missing experience
of the programming language, the use of Python¬ģ improved the quality of the work.
The easier data handling, plotting of graphs and implementing of functions allowed
us to perform better than what we could have achieved using GAMS¬ģ. The different
libraries implemented (besides Gurobi¬ģ and Pandas¬ģ) help to improve the quality of the
displaying of the results (See Seaborn) or to perform fast and complete calculations (See
Numpy). When doubts were arising and difficulties were encountered, the solutions were
almost always available in the forum of the Python¬ģ‚Äôs community[Stack Overflow 2015].
Indeed, being Python¬ģ a programming language frequently used for optimisation scopes,
a lot of examples and improvements for the model were available.
At the end of the project we realised how important it has been to spend part of
our thesis to learn this new tool. Python¬ģ has been useful for the selected purpose and
helped to improve the quality of the results. Besides, the knowledge acquired on the
==PAGE:192==
166
A The appendix
programming language will be useful for future works and experiences, both in and out
from the academic environment.

A.2.2
Wind and Sun
The capacities factors for the wind and sun used are based on the delivered energy after
the market clearing price. In Figure 89 is reported the market clearing price procedure,
according to Nordpool‚Äôs definition :‚ÄĚ12:00 CET - deadline for submitting bids for power
which will be delivered the following day. Elspot feeds the information into a specialist
computer system which calculates the price, based on an advanced algorithm. Put simply,
the price is set where the curves for sell price and buy price meet. Hourly prices are
typically announced to the market at 12:42 CET or later. Once the market prices have
been calculated, trades are settled. From 00:00 CET the next day, power contracts are
physically delivered (meaning that the power is provided to the buyer) hour for hour
according to the contracts agreed‚ÄĚ [Day-ahead market].
The hourly capacity factors for the year are calculated for the period 12-12+90h. The
calculation procedure is illustrated in Figure 90; the data considered are represented by
the black curvy thick line. These represent the forecasted data considering the market
clearing price.
The final data used in the model are found interpolating the single
forecasts for a period of 12h (i.e. the black curvy line reported in Figure 90).
==PAGE:193==
A.2 Data assumptions
167
Figure 89: Market clearing procedure
Calculations
The wind and solar data have been calculated in different ways. The
wind data are taken as an average over the sovereign region of each country, onshore
and where the water depth is less than 70m. Figure 91 is a map where it is shown the
area that each country aggregates over. The x and y axis respectively stand for the
longitude and the latitude.
On the other hand solar data are taken proportionally to the population inside each
grid cell (see Figure 92). This mean that the production is greater where the population
is more dense. Figure 92 displays the contributions from different regions where the
levels are base-10 logarithmically distributed. The colour scale helps to identify the
Figure 90: Selection of Wind and Sun capacity factor
==PAGE:194==
168
A The appendix
Figure 91: Wind map
contributions that range over 4 orders of magnitude. Darker areas have larger installed
capacity and thus contribute more to the capacity factor of the day.
An assumption on the calculation of the wind data is that the wind production
assumes a single 3.5MW turbine which may lower the capacity factor in regions with
low average wind speed. An example can be observed in Figure 93 where the average
Figure 92: Sun map
==PAGE:195==
A.2 Data assumptions
169
capacity for Denmark looked much higher than for the other countries.
Figure 93: Wind plot for the countries
To correct this distortion, series have been normalized to have mean 1 and the
capacity factors afterwards multiplied by the average yearly production of wind in the
country, rather than the country nameplate‚Äôs capacity. Calculations are hereby reported
and explained. For the sun data the same procedure has been followed using adequate
sources ([The Research Council of Norway 2013] [Diane and Fr√©d√©ric 2013])
1. Capacity factors Capacity factors represent the rate of utilization of the technology
(i.e. how much capacity is used compared to the installed capacity) at the given
hours. These were the raw data to be treated.
2. Normalisation The capacity factors were then normalized according to the relation:
x‚Ä≤ =
x
<x>
where x‚Ä≤ is the normalized value, x is the original value and < x > is the average
of all the considered values.
3. Average yearly power production The total yearly power production from
the wind and the sun was found from different sources ([IEA Wind 2013] and
[Wikipedia 2015] respectively).
The average yearly power production for the
different countries was then calculated considering the hours of the year (i.e.8760):
Avg.yearlypow.prod. = totyearlypowprod
8760
==PAGE:196==
170
A The appendix
4. Normalised hourly power production The normalized capacity factors and
average yearly power production from the wind/sun were coupled according to
the relation: Hourly power production = capacity factors * average yearly power
production from wind/sun
The data were then used as input for the upper bound of the wind and sun production
variables.
A.2.3
Marginal cost
The marginal costs used for the simulations has been calculated considering the data
available from the database provided by Dansk Energi. However the database presented
just the electrical efficiency and the variable production costs.
The MC were then
calculated using the relation proposed.
MC=Production costs + Variable production costs e/MWh
The Variable production costs were known while the production costs had to be
calculated. The available electrical efficiency has been used to calculate them. With the
relation

productionscosts
the production costs were therefore calculated. However also the Fuel prices were
unknown. These were extrapolated from Statbank Denmark Statistics Denmark 2015.

A.3

A.3.1

‚ÄĘ Energy production: the hourly energy production for Norway has been collected

‚ÄĘ Time range: a range of eight years have been selected to calculate the average
power production. These are 2006-2013.
‚ÄĘ Hydro capacity installed: the capacity of the hydropower plants for Norway
was calculated considering the database provided by Dansk Energi. It was then
verified through IEA 2015a.

A.3.3
Hydropower considerations
The hydropower production is mainly dependent from three parameters: Inflow, reservoir
level and capacity installed. These can be observed in Figure 109 in a schematic draw
of an hydro power plant.
Figure 109: Example of dam-based hydro power plant
The Inflow represents the amount of water that is flowing into the reservoir. The
reservoir level represent the amount of water that can be found in the reservoir. The
capacity indeed represents the ‚Äôsize‚Äô of the power plant. Being these the only parameters
on which the hydro power production is based (in the model implementation), each of
them was investigated in order to understand the mechanisms behind the technology.
Inflow The inflows given as input in the model follows the typical path of the
Nordic countries: higher during spring and summer because of the snow melting and
lower during the fall and winter. The values of the inflows were analysed in order to
understand if the available data were enough to satisfy the demand of the correspondent
countries. Specifically it was investigated the time period for which the hydro power
plant could run (without getting any refill) before drying out the reservoir level completely.
From the Norwegian TSO it was found that if all the reservoirs are 100 % full, this
would correspond to a potential of 82 TWh [Statnett 2015]. Considering then that the
hydro capacity installed for Norway is 0.03 TW this would mean that Norway could
rely completely on the hydro-power production for almost 114 days, before the reservoir
dries out completely and there is no possibility to produce power through the water. The
same considerations has been done for the other countries and the results are reported
in Table 49. (For the results in the table, the historical values has been used. This led
to a small difference in the final results).
==PAGE:209==
A.3 Hydropower
183
Finland
Norway
Sweden
Max hist reservoir level [MWh]
456800
3781550
3010700
Capacity installed [MW]
305
1515
1631
Days of complete reliability [days]
62.4
103.95

Table 49: Analysis of Hydro power reliability
Analysing the days of complete reliability, it can be concluded that each country
could sustain itself for at least for 2 months without getting any new inflow.
A.3.4
Missing equation on the hydro power formulation
The real mathematical formulation proposed by [Skjelbred 2011] was different than the
one implemented in the hydro base model. The equations were tricked mostly for two
reasons that were connected with the original mathematical formulation: non linearity
of the problem and data treatment. These two will be investigated in the following
sections. The reasons for the non implementation in the model of the original structure
will be thoroughly explained.
A.3.4.1
Mathematical formulation
According to the mathematical formulation proposed by Skjelbred 2011, three equations
should be added to the model in order to calculate the hydro power production:
rgt = rgt‚ąí1 ‚ąíqgt + Igt
‚ąÄt ‚ąąT, ‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro,
(A.4)
htot = hwater + hdrop
‚ąÄt ‚ąąT, ‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro,
(A.5)
pgt = ő∑gqgthtotg
‚ąÄt ‚ąąT, ‚ąÄg ‚ąąGhydro.
(A.6)
Eq.(A.4) represents the relation between the reservoirs level, inflow and outflow of
water. In Eq.(A.5) is reported the specification of the height of the water column. In
Equation A.6 it is then reported the conversion from the flow of water running in the
turbine to power. From the last equation, few parameters are relevant for the conversion
of energy:
‚ÄĘ ő∑g: efficiency of the turbine
‚ÄĘ qg,t: flow of water that is running in the turbine
‚ÄĘ htot:the total height, considering the head of the plant and the level of the water
in the reservoir
‚ÄĘ g: Acceleration of gravity (9, 8
m
s2)
Combining Eq.(A.4) and Eq.(A.6) it results:
ő∑whtotgrw,t = ő∑whtotgrw,t‚ąí1 ‚ąípw,t + Iő∑wghtot
(A.7)
==PAGE:210==
184
A The appendix
that can be summarised as:
ern = en0 ‚ąípw,t + Iő∑wghtot
(A.8)
ern = ő∑whtotgrw,t
(A.9)
en0 = ő∑whtotgrw,t‚ąí1
(A.10)
Last but not least, since the level of the reservoir depends on the Inflow, in situations
in which the inflow of water is zero, Eq.(A.8) will lose the term Iw,t.
The level of the hydro reservoir will therefore be among the boundaries:
rw,t‚ąí1 ‚ąípw,t ‚Č§ern ‚Č§en0 ‚ąípw,t + Iő∑wghtot
(A.11)
Analysing Eq.(A.5)-(A.6) it results clear how the problem would become non linear
with the implementation of these equations. Indeed the height of the water column
is depending on the reservoir level (i.e.hwater). The implementation of these equations
would therefore move the model from linear to non linear.
A.3.4.2
Parameters values
Since the data used for the hydro power plants in the database available from Dansk
Energi¬ģ are considered as an average over the country, all the data useful for the
calculation of hydro power production had to be treated accordingly. Two main values
has been the focus of the analysis to consider ‚Äôaverage values‚Äô. These are the head and the
efficiency of the power plant. An investigation was therefore conducted over more than
hundred power plants available in Norway, Sweden and Finland. The characteristics (i.e.
head and capacity) have been extracted from different sources ([Statkraft 2015], [Andriz
2015], [StatkraftFinland 2015], [Vattenfall 2015] and [GEO 2015]) and grouped in a
database. The considerations are reported in the following sections for each parameter.
Head The characteristics of the hydro power plants have been grouped by country
and analysed. In Figure 110, Figure 111 and Figure 112 can be observed the scatter
distribution of the head of the power plants in function of the capacity installed for the
countries.
==PAGE:211==

Figure 112: Data distribution for Finland
As it can be noticed, the plots showed a random distribution of the data. The next
step was therefore to select a number of cluster around which the distribution of the
data is high and locate the center of it. However, due to the techniques available on the
software (i.e. k-means and random) for the clustering function, it resulted challenging to
decide the number of centroids. The problems was that due to the spacial distribution
of the data, it was difficult to locate the number of clusters. Therefore a more careful
analysis has been conducted in order to validate the choice on the number of cluster.
The technique adopted is known as dendrogram analysis. A dendrogram is a branching
diagram that represents the relationships of similarity among a group of entities. Each
of the branches of the dendrogram are called clade while the terminal end of each clade
is know as leaf.
The arrangement of the clades indicates which leaves are most similar to each other.
On the other hand, the height of the branches indicates how similar or different they are
from each other: the greater the height, the greater the difference. Thus, a dendrogram
can be used for represent the relation between any kind of data.
For the analysis
under discussion, the data anlysed will be the heads and the capacities. In Figure 114,
Figure 115 and Figure 113 the three dendrograms can be observed.

A.3 Hydropower

Efficiency The same considerations were done for the efficiency. It is well know
that there are different kind of turbine, which performs differently depending on the
inflow and the operation at design point. Typical efficiency curves are illustrated in
Figure 119. The values are presented in function of the relative discharge (i.e. the flow
as a percentage of the designed flow).
Figure 119: Typical efficiency curves for different types of hydropower turbines
[Vinogg and Elstad 2003]
From the graph it is clear how different turbines (e.g. Pelton, Crossflow and Kaplan)
presents different efficiencies depending on the inflow considered. The parameters that
affects the efficiency are therefore the kind of turbine that is installed in the plant and
the flow through the turbine. Due to these considerations and the lack of data available
on each power plant installed in the countries considered, it was difficult to decide an
average efficiency to assume for all the hydro power plants.
==PAGE:218==
192
A The appendix
A.3.4.3
Conclusions
The additional equations (See Eq.(A.4)-(A.5)-(A.6)) proposed for the model were analysed
in the particular. Two main problems were found on their implementation:
1. Non linearity of the model: the introduction of the equations would lead the
model to be non linear. Due to the fact that the original idea was to keep the
model simple and formulated as LP, it was decided not to complicate the model.
2. Data assumption: Both the efficiency and the head were found to be difficult
data to treat. Being them two of the main parameters used for the conversion of
power in the hydro technology, whichever use/assumption would have compromised
the results.
For the above-mentioned reasons, it was therefore decided to simplify the model. The
mathematical formulations reported in the base hydro model in Subsection 5.5.4 represent
the final choice.
==PAGE:219==
A.4 Combined heat and power
193
A.4
Combined heat and power
A.4.1
Combined heat and power operation modes
As it has been specified in subsubsection 5.3.4.1, for the condensing and extraction
power plants there is more flexibility when it comes to the simultaneous production of
heat and power. The plant can operate for producing heat or power within the limits
imposed by the feasible region. The latter vary from plant to plant and it depends
on the characteristics of the system. In Weber 2004 it is reported a full description of
the steps to follow in order to build the feasible regions. There are here reported in
order to allow an easier understanding of the assumptions considered while applying the
convex relaxation, used for the plants in the system. The constraints for the creation of
the feasible zone for the backpressure power plants are not reported since the relation
between the heat and power outputs is assumed to be described by a linear relation,
already reported in the main report.
The plant operation can be represented with a P-Q chart which indicates the operation
limits for the combined heat and power production. An example of P-Q chart for a
typical condensing/extraction power plant is reported in Figure 120.
0
20
40
60
80
100
120
140
Heat production Q [MW]
0
50
100
150
200
Power production P [MW]
1
2
3
4
Feasible region
Figure 120: Feasible region for a typical CHP condensing/extraction power plant
All the points included in the feasible regions can be represented by the relation:
PEl,eq = PEl + ő≤HT
ő≤EL Q
(A.12)
where :
==PAGE:220==
194
A The appendix
‚ÄĘ PEl,eq: equivalent electricity production
‚ÄĘ PEl: electricity production
‚ÄĘ Q: heat production
‚ÄĘ
ő≤HT
ő≤EL : electric power reduction due to the heat production
The boundaries of the feasible regions are identified by the technical constrains of
the power plants. The upper bound identified by line 1 represents the maximum steam
output from the boiler. The line can be drawn according to the restriction:
ő≤EL‚Ä≤P EL + ő≤HT ‚Ä≤QHT ‚Č§QSTM
max
(A.13)
On the other hand, the lower bound describe the minimum output from the boiler.
The restriction is identified by line 2 and is identified by:
ő≤EL‚Ä≤‚Ä≤P EL + ő≤HT ‚Ä≤‚Ä≤QHT ‚Č•QSTM
min
(A.14)
Line 3 represent the operation restriction, limited by the maximum amount of
heat that can be produced for a given amount of fuel burnt. Eq(A.15) represent the
restriction:
QHT ‚Č§QHT
max
(A.15)
The last side of the feasible region is identified by the heat to power production ratio.
The restriction is identified by line 4 defined by:
P EL ‚Č•sEL
minQHT
(A.16)
where the parameter sEL
min represent the maximum ratio between the extraction and
condensing flow.
These are the bounds that should define the feasible region according to the technical
parameters of the power plants.
The feasible region will change ‚Äôshape‚Äô and order
according to the different cases investigated.
